HEADLINE: More Than Cars, Not Quite Trucks (but Toyotas to the Core); 
RAV4: Even Grown, It Earns Grins

BYLINE:  By BOB KNOLL 

 BODY:
BEFORE there was a Ford Escape or Hyundai Santa Fe, before the Suzuki Vitara or Chevrolet Tracker, even before there was a Subaru Forester or Honda CR-V, there was the Toyota RAV4. It was among the first of the small, perky, youth-oriented vehicles called cute utes.
 Five years since the RAV4 made its debut, an extensively redesigned model is out. Toyota has significantly beefed up its small sport utility, which is now less of a toy and more of a truly useful utility vehicle. 
 Even in its new, somewhat larger guise -- the 2001 model is 1.3 inches longer and 1.6 inches wider -- the RAV4 is smaller, more lively and more agile than other small sport utilities. The previous two-door model is gone; there is now only a four-door body style with a side-hinged cargo door. Only one engine is available, a new aluminum 2-liter 4-cylinder with 148 horsepower that feels even peppier thanks to its variable valve timing. Either a five-speed manual transmission or four-speed automatic is available, as is front- or all-wheel drive. This is a full-time all-wheel-drive system, like the Highlander's, that splits the power 50-50 to front and rear.
To hold down the base price -- $16,695, including destination charge -- the option list is quite long. All-wheel drive adds $1,400 and an automatic transmission is $1,050. Popular options like air-conditioning, power windows, locks, and cruise control, along with a cassette-plus-CD sound system, are bundled in a $2,295 quick order package.
A fancier L trim package is $3,120, and there are stand-alone options like antilock brakes ($590) and leather interior ($800). In short, you can push the price of this little vehicle to $25,000 and beyond. 
I found the RAV4 to be fun to drive -- a lot more so than the Highlander. Toyotas usually go about their business with such quiet competence that they become, essentially, four-wheeled appliances. The Highlander is that way, but the RAV4 is not.
Its steering response is quick and precise, more so than many sedans'. The responsive engine becomes obtrusively noisy only at high revolutions. The brakes feel tight and progressive, similar to those of my own Audi A4, which I consider to be the standard. The ride is akin to that of a small car, choppy on some roads and smooth on others. In the deep snow that accumulated this winter in New England, the all-wheel-drive test vehicle managed to maintain good traction.
But I was disappointed with the fuel economy. Not only did I expect better than the 22.5 m.p.g. that I got over all, the gauge dropped annoyingly fast because of the rather small, 14.7-gallon tank.
As one might expect, the cabin is narrow. The rear seat is tight for two and very cramped for three. But that seat, which is split in half, folds and can be easily removed. With it out, the 68.3 cubic feet of volume exceeds that of two wagons whose back seats fold but do not come out -- the Subaru Outback (62.1 cubic feet) and Audi A4 Avant (63.7) -- and nearly matches that of the much larger Volvo Cross Country (70).
The RAV4's rear cargo area is exceptionally easy to load and unload because of the low floor and side-opening door. But take heed: there is no rear bumper. Even a slight fender-bender can cause expensive damage. Recent tests by the Institute for Highway Safety, a group financed by the insurance industry, found that rear damage in a 5 m.p.h. collision with a pole or flat barrier would cost more than $2,700 to repair.
The RAV4's instrument panel is simple and straightforward. The climate controls are three simple knobs -- no electronics here. The radio is situated up high and is easy to use. The "champagne colored" gauges are unusual -- their faces look like parchment -- but attractive.
There is the typical safety equipment: front air bags and force-limiting front belts, plus three-point belts for three rear-seat passengers. The outside seat positions in the back have the new Isofix child-seat anchors. 
The previous RAV4 got four stars (of a possible five) for both driver and front passenger in the government's front-crash tests. An Insurance Institute test of the new model -- crashed into a barrier at 40 m.p.h. -- showed improvement. The institute gave the 2001 RAV4 an acceptable rating.
Among small S.U.V.'s, about the same money will buy a RAV4, an Escape, a Santa Fe or a CR-V. But the Honda feels tinny and dated, and the reliability of the Escape and Santa Fe is yet unknown. The RAV4 is still the smallest, but it leads the pack in one respect: It is the most fun to drive.

INSIDE TRACK: Sports car in a box.

GRAPHIC: Photo: RAV4: Quick steering and a peppy engine make it one of the sportiest small S.U.V.'s.

HEADLINE: COPING; 
What Price For a Pet's Life?

BYLINE:  By FELICIA R. LEE 

 BODY:
CHLOE the cat lived a long and mostly good life before dying one February morning. But according to Pamela Gordon, Chloe's owner, what should have been a peaceful ending for a feisty survivor was marred by a series of medical mishaps and an insensitive veterinarian.
 Ms. Gordon, an art dealer who lives in Greenwich Village, found Chloe abandoned in a Paris train station in 1983. She nursed her back to health and good spirits. Chloe was a relatively healthy 19-year-old until early this year. 
 Chloe's heart and kidneys failed in February, sending her into a rapid decline that Ms. Gordon believes could have been prevented if routine blood and urine tests had caught her kidney condition earlier. Ms. Gordon also complained that Chloe's doctor was hard to reach, failed to call in a prescription, gave Chloe the wrong kind of food and dismissed the cat as "aggressive." (The hospital said it had no record of a complaint from Ms. Gordon.)
The grief was bad enough. What was worse, Ms. Gordon said, was that when she began calling lawyers and animal rights groups to redress the injustice of Chloe's death she discovered that little could be done. 
Although she could file a professional complaint against Chloe's veterinarian, nothing in the law allowed her to collect damages for emotional distress and loss of companionship. Most of all, she wanted acknowledgment of the enormity of her loss, Ms. Gordon said. But because pets are considered property under the laws of most states, including New York, Ms. Gordon was advised that her best bet might be to collect the cost of replacing Chloe.
A few judges have ruled in individual cases that losing an animal is not the same as losing other property. But it is an evolving area of law. And awards have tended to be so small that lawyers don't take the cases on contingency. For example, one woman was awarded $700 for her emotional distress after she discovered a strange cat in her dog's coffin right before his burial. The dog had apparently been accidentally cremated.
For that reason, the city's animal rights activists are excited about a bill proposed by Assemblyman Patrick Manning, a Republican from Hopewell Junction, in the Hudson Valley, that would bring state law more in line with the feelings of people who regard their pets as members of the family.
The amendment to the state's Agriculture and Markets Law would make a person liable for up to $5,000 in "non-economic damages if that person kills or injures a pet through an unlawful and intentional reckless or negligent act." The damages, the bill says, "shall be limited to compensation for the loss of reasonably expected society, companionship, love and affection."
Mr. Manning said he proposed the bill because of two recent, highly publicized and malicious dog killings in upstate New York. In one case, a dog was shot in his owner's backyard; in the other, a dog was drowned in a nearby lake. Mr. Manning also heard from animal rights advocates in New York, which has its share of animals killed by the police, garbage collectors and errant bicyclists.
Mostly, Mr. Manning said, he wanted to send a message that animals lives are valued. He said he expected to find a lot of support for his bill in New York, where even studio dwellers are likely to have a cat or two.
Most of the pets living in the city are, like Chloe, companion animals without much "market" value, which means they are not farm animals, actors, models or show animals. The idea of animals as chattel or property goes back to common law. The law has evolved slowly to reflect the reality of pet life in this city, where New Yorkers can take their animals for massage or to luxury pet hotels equipped with television, and to dog clubs with restaurants and agility classes. According to the A.S.P.C.A., there are about 2.8 million households in New York, 1.5 million or so dogs and 1.6 million cats. 
Tennessee last year became the first state to pass legislation recognizing loss of companionship when a pet is killed. A similar bill is pending in Massachusetts. But not surprisingly, the American Veterinarian Service Association, which represent veterinarians, is opposed to broadening laws related to loss of pets. The group says suits for emotional distress will drive up malpractice insurance costs for veterinarians and be passed on in higher costs for pet owners. 
But for Ms. Gordon, money is not the issue.
"We had a wonderful life together," she said of Chloe. "I just wish the end had gone more smoothly. She was my child. She was my baby. When I lost my mother and my grandmother and I had boyfriends who came and went, she was always there for me."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS WORLD; 
A High-Tech Lifeline In Europe's Rust Belt

BYLINE:  By PETER S. GREEN 

DATELINE: WARSAW 

 BODY:
THE factory floor of Z.R.E. Grodek seems an unlikely center of Poland's nascent Internet economy. Its 160 workers spend most of their time forging, banging and welding pieces of metal into boilers, racks, pumps and other parts for the heavy industry that the Internet revolution was supposed to make us forget.
 But unlike dozens of smokestack industries that barely cling to life in formerly Communist countries -- and are undercut by even cheaper labor in Asia and India -- Z.R.E. Grodek has found a new source of business via the Internet, which is slowly making its way onto the factory floors of Central and Eastern Europe. 
 Using a personal computer to dial up a business-to-business Web site in the United States, Z.R.E. Grodek, which was acquired two years ago by Universal Process Equipment Inc. of Robbinsville, N.J., has been bidding on contracts for American manufacturers seeking cheaper sources for labor-intensive products. Currently Z.R.E. (the initials stand for the Polish equivalent of the Electric Equipment Manufacturing Company) is building hundreds of metal frames for moving sheets of glass around a factory. The frames produced in the factory near Swiecie, about a three-and-a-half-hour drive northwest of Warsaw, will be used in American plants by Pilkington, a British glass maker. 
America's love affair with Internet investments soured because of over-optimistic bets on exotic technology applications that failed to produce speedy profits. But there is nothing fancy about Poland's current dot-com prosperity. Electronics entrepreneurs, investors and others have taken a look at the boom and bust of Silicon Valley and the Nasdaq index and focused their efforts and investments on bringing clicks to bricks, adapting traditional business to the Internet.
They have avoided most of the business-to-consumer models that crashed in America and are still a pipe dream in Poland, where only an estimated 5 to 8 percent of the population of 39 million has regular Internet access. And much of that is through old, unreliable phone lines.
"You can't make money on the Internet if there's no Internet in the first place," said Henning Esskuchen, an analyst at Erste Bank, an Austrian bank that is active in Central Europe. "What we think is doable and will survive in terms of start-ups are companies that focus on Internet infrastructure or on bringing existing companies to the Web."
Poland's overall economy remains one of the strongest in the region, growing 4.1 percent in 2000, and its sober entry into high technology should help the country in its quest to catch up to the living standards of Western Europe. Portals are opening, old media are moving online, inventors and developers are offering new products and new companies are still attracting investors. Industry analysts say investment in Poland's Internet sector is in the hundreds of millions of dollars.
In the last five years, 15 Polish high-technology companies have been listed on the Warsaw Stock Exchange, though the market has not been kind to them recently. The exchange generally follows Nasdaq trends when it comes to technology shares, even if the Polish economy is not very dependent on the Internet. The 19-stock TechWIG index of Polish high-technology shares has fallen 42 percent since it was created in May 2000.
For now, Internet-related and information technology companies account for only a small percentage of the nation's economic activity, said Benita Mikolajewicz, an equity analyst at ING Barings in Warsaw. "Most of the Internet activity was done by traditional companies as a supplement to their main activity," she added.
That means opportunities for Poland's handful of high-technology venture capitalists. Rafal Styczen first made his fortune -- he declines to reveal its size -- six years ago, when, at 24, he floated his first high-technology company on the Warsaw exchange. The company, ComArch, in Krakow, designs and assembles computer systems for businesses. Last year, he started the Internet Investment Fund, also based in Krakow, to invest in Poland's new economy.
Mr. Styczen's fund has started nine companies. New investors have already bought interests in two of them, and two others are now operating profitably. The lack of stock market exposure has allowed Polish Internet companies to develop sensibly, he said, and to make their money by selling products and services, not shares.
"The hype was not here," Mr. Styczen said. "Internet companies didn't have time to raise a lot of money before the market went down."
Among his ventures is Billbird (www.mojerachunki.pl), which automates Poland's antiquated bill-paying system, a process that often requires payers to spend hours in line at post offices with wads of cash. SynergiXmedia, another venture, creates and sells interactive Web sites. Other investments include E-Center in Krakow, which provides a home, hardware and technical support for Web site operators, and Interactive Medicine, an online service for dentists to handle cumbersome bookkeeping and to manage orders for drugs and equipment.
Curiously, it is Poland's less-developed financial system that has helped the country's high-technology sector avoid much of the frenzied overextension that undermined many Silicon Valley start-ups.
POLAND has no masses of individual investors. Few people have significant savings, and interest rates on bank loans hover near 20 percent, making investing a luxury. And because investment dollars are scarce, Poland's fledgling Internet ventures have had to make a strong case for financing and have yet to let their growth get out of hand.
Venture capital from the United States, Britain and even from large Polish businesses like Elektrim S.A., a telecommunications conglomerate partly owned by Vivendi Universal of France, are all investing heavily in Polish high-technology businesses.
"The quality of business plans and new opportunities in the region is growing," says Marcin Hejka, strategic investments manager for Eastern Europe at Intel Capital, a division of the Intel Corporation, the world's largest maker of computer chips.
For the most part, Mr. Hejka said, money is still available. "If a company has a good idea, the right team and the right product, it shouldn't have trouble attracting venture capital investors" in Poland, he added.
Among the advantages that Poland and its neighbors offer to high-technology entrepreneurs is an abundance of smart, relatively low-paid engineers, Mr. Hejka said. One of Intel's investments is in NC Virtual Systems, based in Warsaw, which says it has solved a major problem for the mobile telephone industry -- how to cram enough data over the airwaves so that a portable device like a mobile phone can function almost as well as a desktop computer.
Such software could vastly accelerate the introduction of mobile Internet services that are now held back by slow operating systems and the limited capacity of the airwaves to carry the huge amounts of data needed to bring high-speed data transmissions to mobile phones. And that could mean vast profits for NC and its backers.
Banks, insurance companies and other service companies in Poland are also moving more of their operations online. And while media companies in Europe and the United States are cutting back on Internet investments, Agora A.S., the parent of Poland's largest and most profitable daily newspaper, Gazeta Wyborcza, is betting $10 million that its established brand will give it an edge as an Internet portal.
Filled with how-to information for consumers, Agora's Web site, www.gazeta.pl, will also have a database of the newspaper's archives, including reports from its 20 regional editions and its vast collection of classified ads.
Agora faces a challenge from I.T.I. Holdings, a Polish media group that recently bought 25 percent of the Polish computer services company Optimus and its Internet portal, Onet. I.T.I. already owns the nationwide television station TVN and four multiplex movie theaters.
Poland's Internet market clearly has room to grow, particularly as Internet use slowly climbs closer to Western European levels, said Ms. Mikolajewicz of ING Barings. In fact, Poland's style of dot-com entrepreneurship won a vote of confidence last week when Softbank Emerging Markets Inc., a $200 million venture capital fund created by the Softbank Corporation of Japan and a unit of the World Bank, opened a Central Europe office in Warsaw to invest in high-technology and Internet ventures in Central and Eastern Europe.
"What is developing in Central Europe is in many cases going to leapfrog developments elsewhere, and the market is just starting," said Matt Rothman, the chief executive of Softbank Emerging Markets. "So a lot of things that were tried and rejected in the U.S. will be avoided here, and you'll come up with more appropriate and localized solutions that I think will have a lot of success."

HEADLINE: INVESTING WITH/Val Jensen; 
Jensen Portfolio

BYLINE:  By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:
CAN a book change a life? Absolutely, says Val Jensen, lead manager of the $40 million Jensen Portfolio.
 In 1979, William E. Fruhan Jr., a Harvard Business School professor, wrote "Financial Strategy: Studies in the Creation, Transfer and Destruction of Shareholder Value." Mr. Fruhan studied 1,500 companies from 1965 to 1975 and found common characteristics among 72 outstanding performers: a focused business, excess capital and a sustained competitive advantage. Mr. Jensen was so impressed that nearly 17 years later, most of those 72 companies are still on his short list of stocks that he considers owning. 
 "We got 10 copies of the book but we gave most of them away," Mr. Jensen said from his office in Portland, Ore. "It did change our world. It's the foundation of our business."
The philosophical framework has proved solid for investors. The fund returned 11.7 percent a year, on average, for the three years ended Thursday, compared with 5.6 percent for its peers in the large-growth category and 5 percent for the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, according to Morningstar Inc.
It lost just 2.3 percent during the difficult 12 months ended Thursday, compared with a 25.4 percent loss for its group and a 14.5 percent drop in the S.& P. 500. Jensen Investment Management, the fund's investment adviser, also manages about $160 million in individual accounts.
A company's cash is king for Mr. Jensen.
"We look for companies that have consistently generated cash earnings for their shareholders," he said. "Unlike reported earnings, cash earnings are available to management to reward shareholders by doing things like paying down debt, declaring dividends or buying back shares." To figure cash earnings, he adds back noncash charges like depreciation but subtracts capital expenditures.
Mr. Jensen, 71, works closely with a four-member investment committee to select the 20 or so companies in the fund from a list of about 90 to 115 that meet his investment principles. 
His primary screen looks for excess capital. Specifically, that means a minimum return on equity of at least 15 percent a year for 10 consecutive years -- a goal that can be met only by companies that have a sustained competitive advantage, he said. Most of the businesses generate at least 70 percent of total revenue from a single product or service.
He also wants significant free cash flow, which he defines as the cash left after operating expenses, including maintenance for capital assets. Many of the qualifying companies also have high profit margins -- 10 percent to 20 percent or more.
A main part of his balance-sheet analysis is trends in inventory and accounts receivable. "We like to see inventory and receivables growing at the same rate as revenues," he said. "That tells us that the company is not using accounting maneuvers to inflate their earnings or booking income from one-time sources."
Mr. Jensen also reviews changes in the number of shares outstanding, using share buybacks as a proxy for shareholder-friendly management.
He strays from Mr. Fruhan's teaching in one area: valuation. Mr. Fruhan said outstanding companies were often expensive; Mr. Jensen looks for companies that sell for significant discounts to what he believes they are worth.
Specifically, he buys shares at discounts of 50 to 60 percent of what he considers their intrinsic value. He calculates that value by adding the present value of a company's estimated cash flow over the next 10 years to what he believes the company's assets will be worth the year after that.
He generally trims positions when share prices reach 100 percent to 130 percent of their intrinsic value.
IN January, Mr. Jensen bought shares of Emerson Electric at an average cost of $73.86. Emerson, the electrical equipment maker based in St. Louis, has reported record earnings for 43 years in a row, he said. Its annual return on equity has averaged 19.7 percent in the last 10 years.
"It's a beautifully managed company," Mr. Jensen said. "They are able to read their customers well enough to know what business they should concentrate on." Moreover, management has been buying back stock aggressively, increasing dividends and making small acquisitions. "They do almost everything we want with their free cash flow," he said, adding that shares were relatively cheap because of investors' general worries about electronics makers in a slowing economy.
On Friday, Emerson shares closed at $67.14; Mr. Jensen figures their intrinsic value at $112.
Mr. Jensen has been adding shares of Gannett, the largest newspaper group in the country. He first bought shares in 1995 at an average of $26.57. The company's return on equity has averaged 20.8 percent in the last decade, he said.
The stock now sells at a large discount, he added, figuring that "with a slowdown in the economy, people are concerned about declining advertising." But he called the stock a bargain over the longer term. "Gannett is a very well-operated company," he said, "so if the economy continues to grow, Gannett is in a perfect place because of its strong franchise."
The stock is now at $65.87; Mr. Jensen calculates its intrinsic value at $139.
Another favorite is the State Street Corporation, in Boston, which he originally bought in 1998 at an average of $70.81 a share. "It's covered by bank analysts," he said, "but it's hardly a bank anymore." 
Last year, State Street sold its last retail banking business; it is now engaged solely in custodial, record-keeping and other services for mutual funds and institutional investors. State Street has roots that go back to 1792; dividends have been paid every year since. The shares are inexpensive, he said, because of Wall Street concerns that earnings will decline over the next few quarters. His view is different. 
"Earnings won't fall," he said, "but they won't increase as quickly as Wall Street would like."
On Friday, the shares closed at $104.50; he said their intrinsic value was $162.

HEADLINE: FIVE QUESTIONS/for NEIL E. HARL; 
Converging Forces Afflict Farms

BYLINE:  By DAVID BARBOZA 

 BODY:
IT'S not easy to be a farmer these days. The agriculture sector has been in a recession for nearly four years. Commodity prices are depressed. Debate is intensifying over genetically altered crops and over the mergers of giant food processors into ever bigger corporations that demand large-scale precision farming. In Europe, there is mad cow disease and foot-and-mouth disease. At home, there is flooding along the Mississippi. And Congress and the Bush administration appear to be haggling over whether farmers will require another bailout this year. 
 Neil E. Harl, the Charles F. Curtiss professor of agriculture at Iowa State University, has been writing and teaching about agriculture for nearly 40 years and has been an adviser to state and federal agencies. He talked last week about the challenges confronting American farms.
 DAVID BARBOZA

Q. Corn prices have fallen 10 percent in the last month, to their lowest level in decades. Why are American farmers expected to plant so much corn this year, if prices are so depressed?

A. First of all, individual farmers don't make decisions based on the aggregate picture. They make decisions that are the best for them. This isn't Intel or Boeing. They think about aggregate market impact; no farmer is big enough to do that.
Secondly, they have fixed costs. So long as they can more than cover their variable costs, they keep on producing. You might say, "Why not try some other crop?" Well, every crop is in the tank. But they always hope that the next year will be better. In football and farming, there's always next year.
And there's government support as a motivating force. Last year, more than half of the net farm income came from government support. For some, it was all from the government. And they have expectations it'll come again this year. Last year, the government paid out a record $28 billion in direct payments. Not even in the 80's were the payments that large.

Q. If the farm economy is so depressed, why haven't supermarket prices fallen noticeably?

A. One factor is that the farmer's share of the retail food dollar is relatively small, particularly for processed foods. The costs incurred beyond the farm gate have a great influence on retail prices. Another thing that's been happening is massive concentration in the retail food market.
In 1992, the five leading food chains had 19 percent of grocery sales. In 1998, they had about 33 percent; and it was estimated at 42 percent last year with some projections of 60 percent soon. So what we are seeing is an astounding amount of concentration.
A good laboratory for the problems was in 1998-99, when we saw hog prices fall to 8 cents a pound. We think reduced competition alters the way retailers handle their pricing practices. I asked one retailer why didn't they drop their prices. They said: "Why should we? The product is moving off the shelves."
There has been a change in the way food is retailed. There's less sensitivity in the way things are priced. Retailers move because of competition and because of pressure. Concentration and vertical integration are a deadly combination.

Q. There have been some government projections this year that crop and livestock prices will strengthen and farm income will climb. And the Bush administration has indicated that it wants to defer any decision on emergency bailout money for distressed farmers, while Congress wants billions of dollars in emergency aid. How do you explain that?

A. I think they both know there's going to be a need for funding. I think the Bush administration wants to reduce the amount of support, so they don't want to lock it into the budget. They want to minimize the outlays and hope something happens to make it less necessary. The Food and Agriculture Policy Research Institute a group based at Iowa State projected reduced farm incomes recently without emergency assistance. They have shown we should expect a significant decrease in farm incomes.

Q. The Mississippi River is threatening to flood parts of the farming heartland. But the threat doesn't seem to be affecting price projections or crop forecasts. Why?

A. The reason is that such a small proportion of the cropland is involved. There are levees all along much of the Mississippi. And even if those were breached, the amount of land inundated would be modest.
Plus, it's still early enough in the growing season for some late-planted crops to be put in. It's unlikely those phenomena will have a significant impact on farm production.

Q. Has the livestock crisis in Europe resulted in larger U.S. exports of meat to the European Union?

A. You would think all the destruction of animals in Europe would lead to more consumption of American meat, and it has, to a degree. But it is probably changing eating habits in Europe. Some may shift to poultry and seafood. On a short-term basis, the demand for meat has probably dropped. And if there is demand for meat, the supply will probably come from Europe and elsewhere. There's still quite a bit of production in Europe.
We are a potential supplier but we face two significant barriers. The E.U. still bars hormone-treated beef from the U.S., despite an adverse W.T.O. decision, so that limits our ability to fill those gaps. In addition, U.S. poultry exports to the E.U. are limited because the two governments have not yet resolved all poultry issues in a veterinary equivalency agreement, under which each recognizes the other's meat inspection systems as scientifically equivalent.

SECTION: Section 3; Column 2; Money and Business/Financial Desk; Pg. 1

LENGTH: 3464 words

HEADLINE: Workers, and Bosses, in a Visa Maze 

BYLINE:  By LESLIE WAYNE 

DATELINE: ISELIN, N.J. 

 BODY:
AT the Delhi Darbar restaurant, where the Indian expatriate community here comes to eat, the talk is not about the food, but about the uncertainty of life in America these days. Gathered around the table are four computer programmers, born in India and educated at top schools there, who now work in high-technology jobs in the New York area. Along with their wives and children, they came here nearly six years ago, on the promise of a job paying 10 times more than they could earn back home and the expectation of pursuing the American dream.
 But they cannot. The four -- and up to 420,000 others like them -- are caught up in a labyrinth of immigration laws, Congressional politics, the competing forces of the high-tech industry and organized labor and a tortuous, dysfunctional process of gaining "green cards" that allow permanent residency here. As a result, while they write the Java computer programs or manage the C++ computer projects that American business needs, they are never quite sure which end of the earth -- the United States or India -- they should call home. 
 "All of us are in limbo," said Shailesh Gala, one of the four, as they munched on chicken tikka and curries. "They created a false promise for us. Everyone who comes here has worked hard. We are waiting. Waiting. Waiting. Waiting, and nothing is happening." As heads nodded in agreement, another programmer, Bhushan Sethi, said: "We are migrating through legal ways. We are here when the country needed us. But when we need this country, it is not there. Give us the opportunity to stay."
The companies that hire these workers, called H-1B's for the visa that lets them work for six years while seeking green cards, say much the same. From Microsoft to Intel, from Goldman Sachs to Merrill Lynch, from Deloitte Consulting to Ernst & Young, H-1B's are being hired by the planeload to fill a gap in computer skills, especially at higher levels, because Americans have been cool to the computer science and engineering education that foreign students embrace.
The H-1B visa program has its champions. High-tech superstars like Bill Gates, Andy Grove and Scott McNealy have lobbied Congress to expand it. And holders of H-1B's, besides many faceless programmers, have included Linus Torvalds, 31, the Finnish inventor of the Linux operating system, a software rival to Microsoft's Windows, who recently traded his visa for a green card. 
But as the program has grown, so have its problems -- as even its backers acknowledge. 
Both a guest worker program and a road to a green card, the H-1B visa system has become a lightning rod of controversy over immigration policy. High-tech companies say the program is so riddled with bureaucratic delays that they have had to hire whole staffs to keep everything straight. The period for getting green cards now stretches for years, keeping the lives of H-1B's -- and the plans of corporations -- up in the air. After investing in and training an H-1B employee for up to six years, a company may well see him sent packing on the next airplane out. 
Moreover, under pressure from organized labor, Congress enacted complicated rules making it hard for corporations to promote H-1B workers or reward them with more challenging assignments.
The government isn't happy, either. Three agencies -- the Labor and State Departments and the Immigration and Naturalization Service -- administer the program, leading to regulatory gridlock and, according to a blistering report from the General Accounting Office, a breakdown in federal oversight.
As a result, nearly everyone involved with the program -- supporters and detractors -- complains about its flaws, often using words like "disaster" and "train wreck" as descriptions.
"The H-1B system needs to work for everybody and now it works for no one," said Jenifer Eisen Verdery, manager of education and work-force policy at Intel, the fifth-largest employer of H-1B's. "It doesn't work for the immigrant who has it. It doesn't work for the employers who hire them. It doesn't work for agencies that run it."
The H-1B program has its roots in temporary visa programs begun in the 1950's. But it took off in the 1980's and, again, in the late 1990's, as high-tech industries blossomed. 
Late last year, with hefty campaign contributions and a lobbying juggernaut, employers persuaded Congress to increase the number of H-1B visas granted annually to 195,000. And estimates show that the total number of H-1B's here could reach a high of 710,000 over the next three years. Compared with a national work force of 140 million, that number is minuscule. But it is a sizable and growing minority in information technology, where up to 20 percent of the five million high-tech workers are foreign-born, with about half of those coming from the H-1B program. It is too early to tell what impact, if any, the current economic downturn will have on the numbers.
HIGH-TECH companies say they have no choice but to hire H-1B's. "Of course, we make every effort to find skills within U.S. workers," said Zoanne Hennigan, director of immigration at Intel, which hires H-1B's as software, network and component design engineers. "But when there is a skill shortage, H-1B has been absolutely instrumental to making sure our business goals are met. The H-1B program is critical to the success of our company."
Such talk, however, holds little sway with organized labor and American-born computer programmers who have thrown obstacles in the program's way for the last decade. They contend that the program is just a way for high-tech companies, which are largely not unionized, to get programmers on the cheap, hold them captive in jobs where they have little voice over working conditions and jettison them when they are no longer needed. 
These groups have been among the most vocal in pointing out H-1B abuses -- cases of underpayment, fraud and exploitation of a vulnerable, compliant work force. But while waving the banner for worker protection, organized labor has succeeded only in convincing Congress to enact measures that have made the system more byzantine -- and unintentionally made it more open to potential abuse. For example, the paperwork required of high-tech companies to show that they have not replaced American citizens with H-1B's has become so onerous that, as a practical matter, it makes it difficult for H1-B's to change jobs.
"There isn't anything I like about the program," said Paul E. Almeida, president of the Department of Professional Employees at the A.F.L.-C.I.O. "It looks to me like it is just used to suppress wages. These employers don't want to invest in workers if they don't have to. This opens the door to low-wage workers." 
SOME American programmers, who have started Web sites opposed to H-1B, share this fervor. One well-known site, created by John Miano, head of the Programmers Guild, a trade group, features banner headlines like "Slave Trade Still Alive in the U.S." 
"Most H-1B's are blithering incompetents," said Mr. Miano, adding that he has worked alongside H-1B's and has had to correct their mistakes. "They are unbelievably bad. They are not highly skilled, it's laughable. This is not a program for the best and the brightest. Anyone could be packaged by a lawyer and get an H-1B."
Whether a shortage of high-tech workers truly exists -- statistics are inconclusive -- H-1B visa holders are largely highly educated, government reports show. Many are educated at India's well-established engineering schools and, increasingly, at American universities. 
Almost 98 percent of them have bachelor's degrees or higher, and 41.5 percent have master's degrees or higher. The jobs generally pay $45,000 to $150,000 -- versus a more typical $6,000 for similar jobs in India, the biggest source of supply. (While the vast majority of H-1B's are in high-tech industries, a small number of the visas are issued for teachers, medical personnel, artists, fashion models and others.) 
About one-quarter of high-tech H-1B's were already here before getting their visas, typically attending American universities on student visas. Government data from 1998, the most recent available, shows that 35 percent of all high-tech master's degrees at American universities went to foreign nationals, as did about half of all high-tech doctorates. 
In Silicon Valley, many companies recruit these foreign students -- many of whom would otherwise have to leave -- right after graduation. Corporations also send recruiters to India, find workers through Internet job postings or bring them here from previous projects overseas. And some companies, both in high technology and on Wall Street, hire H-1B's from consulting firms working as subcontractors on specific technical projects. 
"The H-1B program takes the creme de la creme," said Sheela Murthy, an immigration lawyer in Owings Mills, Md., and a native of India. "These are not people who are coming here to clean toilets. Many of them have master's degrees and attended top-notch universities. They are coming with a dream and a desire to do something with their lives."
Yet in accepting the visas, high-tech workers often walk into a nightmare. The visa allows workers and their families to stay here for six years, but the lag in getting green cards has grown to four, five or six years. As a result, thousands of H-1B holders, many of whom filed for green cards immediately upon receiving their H-1B's, are bumping against that six-year limit. Some may receive one-year extensions, but others may be forced to leave.
Employers are equally anxious. "An employer has found a person and wants them to stay, and what then, after six years you have to let them go?" said Theresa Cardinal Brown, associate director of the American Immigration Lawyers Association.
The wait for green cards results not only from delays at the immigration service, which now has 900,000 people in its green-card backlog, but also from supply-and-demand imbalances. At a time when the United States is admitting up to 195,000 new H-1B's annually, there are only 140,000 employment-based green cards available annually for H-1B's -- and their spouses, who are usually admitted on a different visa, an H-4. 
There are few specific criteria for obtaining a green card -- an employer must simply certify that the applicant has needed skills, and applicants must not have criminal records. The I.N.S. keeps no statistics on the number of H-1B's who eventually get green cards. 
The squeeze is tightest for citizens of India and China, who represent 57 percent of all H-1B's. Until recently, those two nations were each allotted 9,800 green cards, the same number set aside for citizens of any other country -- for instance, Iceland or Belgium. Late last year, Congress lifted those quotas, allowing for the transfer of unused allocations from small countries to larger ones. But regulations to put the changed law into effect are still in the works.
"The H-1B program is mismanaged," said B. Lindsay Lowell, an immigration specialist at Georgetown University. "People don't think through the connections between the temporary visa program and permanent residency, and it leaves people in these hazily defined roles. There is backlog on backlog. We are increasing the numbers willy-nilly in a system that cannot process the paperwork, and it is a disaster."
By contrast, other countries, particularly Canada, are actively wooing these same workers. Starting in 1998, Canada began to speed up its processing of high-tech workers, allowing them to gain permanent residency in a matter of months if they score enough points on a ranking that considers their education and the needs of the country. Australia and New Zealand have adopted similar changes, and England, Ireland, Germany and Austria are relaxing their rules, too. 
THE I.N.S. is aware of the competition: "Canada has made it a national policy to increase their market share of high-tech workers," said Jacquelyn Bednarz, a special policy assistant at the I.N.S. "They are courting them with attractive ads. They are welcoming them and aggressively pursuing them."
For H-1B's in the United States, the protracted process has practical consequences: They must pay taxes, including Social Security, with no assurance of ever receiving the future benefits. Their spouses are not allowed to work. They raise children as Americans, even though they may, one day, have to go to a country that is foreign to them. 
Because the visa is issued for a specific job, many H1-B's fear that they will hurt their chances of getting green cards if they try to switch employers, despite new rules allowing them to do that. As a result, until they get their green cards, many H-1B's remain tied to their first employers. 
"The bond between me and my employer is stronger than between me and my wife," said Murali Krishna, who works in Silicon Valley. "Break the umbilical cord and let me come and work for who I want to and negotiate what I can get. That way employers can't exploit me. If you want to be legal, you cannot be ambitious and grow."
Similarly, as a practical matter, it is impossible for H-1B's to become high-tech entrepreneurs, no matter how much brain power and drive they may have, because they would lose their green-card sponsors. "I have great business ideas, but I cannot start a company," said Mr. Sethi, one of those dining at the Delhi Darbar restaurant. "The environment in the U.S. makes you think about becoming an entrepreneur. But this process cuts you down."
Many H-1B's say they live in a constant fear of being deported, should they lose their jobs. The I.N.S. has said it plans to be lenient in the current high-tech downturn, but if an H-1B worker is out of work for more than 10 days, he is "out of status" and can be told to leave immediately. While the I.N.S. has had no reports of deportations recently, serious life decisions, like buying a home and car, having children, and firming up a career, are made more difficult by such uncertainty.
Consider S. Samuels, an H-1B computer programmer who spoke on condition that his first name not be used. He came to the United States to get an M.B.A., after graduating from India's top engineering school, the Indian Institute of Technology. That was more than five years ago. Now, with a pregnant wife and one child who knows only of life here, he is worried.
"If a citizen or a green-card holder loses their job, they lose time or money," Mr. Samuels said. "For us, we can lose our whole life."
Moreover, the lengthy green-card process makes H-1B's much more vulnerable to abuse and exploitation, like underpayment or a practice called "benching," in which H-1B's working for computer consulting firms are not paid for time spent waiting between assignments. Many of these consulting firms, also called "body shops," act as middlemen, hiring H-1B's and sending them on temporary assignments for Fortune 500 companies.
While reports of "body shop" abuse are common among H-1B's, it is hard to come up with hard evidence, since workers fear being sent back to their home country if they challenge their employer. But some have. 
Earlier this month, in response to a suit filed by an H1-B worker, a California superior court ordered a Silicon Valley consulting firm, Compubahn, to drop a restrictive noncompete clause requiring H-1B's to pay the firm $25,000 if they quit. The firm is appealing. H-1B's have complained in computer chat rooms about this practice elsewhere. 
Fraud in obtaining visas has also been found, including one tragic case in Berkeley, Calif., in which three Indian girls were brought in on H-1B visas for sexual purposes. The case came to light in November 1999 after one of the three, a 17-year-old pregnant girl, died of carbon monoxide poisoning in the rented apartment the girls shared. Last month, the landlord, Lakireddy Reddy, 63, a native of India worth more than $50 million, pleaded guilty in federal court in Oakland, as did two of his relatives. His two sons have also been charged in the case, in which the group is accused of fraudulently bringing Indian nationals to the United States for cheap labor and sex.
BEYOND the headlines, it is difficult to determine how much abuse has occurred. By most accounts, it is a small but troubling aspect of the program. In the 2000 fiscal year, the Department of Labor received 140 complaints from H-1B workers and ordered back wages of $1.6 million to 339 people. But the statistics may understate the problem, because H-1B workers' dependence on their employers may make them reticent. "There is a huge disincentive for H-1B's to complain," said a labor department enforcement official. "They are very unwilling to come to us."
One advocate of a speedier green-card process is Mr. Torvalds, the inventor of the Linux computer operating system, who received his green card five months ago. Mr. Torvalds, who lives in Silicon Valley and is chief scientist at the Transmeta Corporation, which sponsored him for a green card, was luckier than most -- he came from Finland, a country with no waiting list. 
Yet even he faced the same difficulties as many other H-1B's. He paid cash for his home to avoid problems in getting a mortgage; he was put in the "problem queue" at United States Customs whenever returning from overseas trips, adding hours to his journey; and it took him more than nine months to get a California driver's license because of the need for immigration paperwork.
"People who end up coming on the program are pretty much at the mercy of the system," Mr. Torvalds said in an interview by e-mail. "That makes people nervous. It can cause total disruption in your life if the six-year clock runs out and you have to go back to a life you've long since left behind. That uncertainty is the nasty kind. 
"Right now," he added, "the whole process is nothing but bureaucracy, with nobody driving it, and nobody really responsible."
On the front lines of many of these problems are employers, who pay $1,000 for the visa, an additional $3,500 to $5,000 in legal fees and untold dollars in employee time to hire H-1B's. Under pressure from organized labor, Congress enacted a cap in 1990 to limit the number of H-1B visas. And, in the mid-1990's, labor pressed Congress to require that all H-1B employers file a "labor condition application" with the Labor Department to protect American workers from being displaced.
The paperwork for this application has grown; the regulations for it now run to 150 pages. At a minimum, an employer must show he has advertised the job to the local work force, is not paying an H-1B any more or any less than comparable workers, is not using H-1B's as strikebreakers and has sought to hire an American citizen first. As a result, the hiring of an H-1B can take months. 
One Wall Street firm has employees who are designated to process H-1B's, including counseling for the bosses of H-1B workers to understand the complications. "They have no idea of the intricacies of the process," said a representative of the firm, which asked not to be named. "And explaining it can last up to a couple of hours."
This mountain of paperwork also discourages companies from promoting H-1B workers or transferring them to another job site. 
"One of the major problems is that an employee is locked into a post," said Sandra J. Boyd, a lobbyist for the National Association of Manufacturers, a trade group. "If they want to move up the ladder, all this paperwork has to start again."
SOME employers, especially those in small companies, have even adopted a no-H-1B policy. A search of popular Internet high-tech employment sites like www.Dice.com finds dozens of solicitations for software engineers with the following designation: "U.S. citizens and green-card holders only." Calls to recruiters placing these ads resulted in the same responses -- that many employers do not want to face the costs, paperwork or delays that come with hiring an H-1B. 
"A lot of companies want someone right away," said one recruiter, who spoke on condition of anonymity. "Time kills these deals."
So does money. Last December, Congress increased H-1B visa fees from $500 to $1,000, to pay for government-sponsored job training programs for high-tech workers. In the current fiscal year, the Department of Labor estimates that it will receive $180 million for these training programs and $213 million next year. 
But will this money go toward training United States citizens to take the highly-skilled jobs now filled by H-1B's? It is hard to say. The training program is still in its infancy. So far, a large portion of the money is going into basic programs to train disadvantaged youths, single parents and the unemployed in entry-level computer jobs -- jobs not filled by H-1B's, but largely by American workers.

PUB-DATE: April 29, 2001
 Late Edition - Final 

SECTION: Section 4; Column 5; Week in Review Desk; Pg. 4

LENGTH: 943 words

HEADLINE: The Nation; 
The Check Is In the Mail. Almost.

BYLINE:  By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON 

 BODY:
THE economy has been looking shaky lately, and like it or not, Congress is on the case.
 Eager to do their part to keep the long expansion alive, members of both parties agree that they should put a substantial amount of money into the hands of consumers this year through an immediate tax cut that would supplement the longer-term plan championed by President Bush. 
 A few months ago, they were talking about a tax cut for 2001 of as little as $6 billion, an amount that would be little more than symbolic in an economy that produces $10 trillion of goods and services annually. But even though it remains unclear how dire the outlook is -- on Friday the government reported that the economy expanded at a reasonably healthy 2 percent annual rate in the first three months of the year -- the stimulus package has ballooned to somewhere between $60 billion and $85 billion, enough to make a modest difference. 
The problem now is that Congress can't agree on how to give the money away.
It could mail out one-time rebate checks. It could cut income tax rates and encourage workers to reduce the amount of money they have withheld from their paychecks by a corresponding amount. It could declare a holiday on payroll taxes -- those deductions for Social Security and Medicare. Or it could try something really unorthodox, like cutting capital gains taxes to energize the stock market, an idea floated this month by Republicans who think the key to prosperity is held by the shell-shocked investor class. 
But each option has serious practical complications. Each is also saddled with political or ideological baggage that has contributed to the partisan divisions over how best to proceed. The difficulty of reaching agreement underscores one of the central questions about how government should address the ups and downs of the business cycle: whether tax policy is always going to be so slow-moving that it can never be an effective anti-recession tool, especially when contrasted with the Federal Reserve's ability to slash interest rates quickly. 
"Even if Congress works at breakneck speed, it's hard to imagine anyone having a check in their hands before July or August at the earliest, and by then we may be past the worst," said Bruce Bartlett, a senior fellow at the National Center for Policy Analysis, a conservative research group. 
Just because the worst might be past (though that is by no means certain) doesn't mean a recovering economy couldn't benefit from the pick-me-up that a tax cut would provide. But what kind of tax cut? 
Like many Democrats, Senator Joseph I. Lieberman of Connecticut favors sending 200 million taxpayers -- people who are paying payroll or income taxes -- a one-time $300 check, an approach that would cost $60 billion. Supporters say the rebate is simple and, because it gives the same amount to everyone, progressive. But economists have come to a consensus that one-time rebates are not a very efficient form of stimulus. They cite research that shows that people tend to save a relatively large portion of any money they consider a one-time windfall, rather than spend it. And getting consumers to spend is the whole point of the exercise. 
The data suggests "that making a tax cut explicitly one-shot rather than continuing considerably reduces but does not eliminate its effect on spending," said Alan S. Blinder, an economics professor at Princeton. Mr. Blinder studied a number of cases, including the last big rebate, in 1975, when Congress and President Ford agreed to send out one-time checks for 10 percent of the previous year's tax liability for each worker, up to $200 per person. He found that a temporary change in tax rates yielded only about half the stimulative bang for the buck of a permanent tax cut, and that a rebate yielded only about 38 percent as much of an impact. 
But if a permanent tax change packs a more immediate economic punch, what form should it take? Some Democrats are pushing to cut the lowest income tax rate to 10 percent from 15 percent. Their approach would put more money in the hands of all taxpayers, since everyone's income is taxed at least partly at the lowest rate. But it would have a more noticeable impact on lower-income workers, since they would presumably need the money more than wealthier people and be more likely to spend it. 
Most Republicans, though, insist that all rates, not just the lowest bracket, should be cut. Such a move would mean that people with the biggest tax bills would get the biggest tax cut -- a matter of simple fairness for Republicans. Democrats object on both ideological and economic grounds. There is no reason to give a big tax cut to the wealthy when it is lower-income people who are most at risk from the slowdown, they say. 
As a practical matter, any immediate tax cut has become intertwined with Mr. Bush's plan. In part by focusing attention on the need for tax stimulus this year, Democrats have drained some support away from the president's proposal. Mr. Bush conceded last week that he would not get the full $1.6 trillion he had requested for the decade starting next year, and he probably will not even get the $1.4 trillion he floated as a compromise. 
Whatever form the stimulus eventually takes, it is probably wise not to assume that its restorative powers will cure the economy. "The problem with all these proposals is that the notion that tax policy this month can stimulate the economy next month is unrealistic at best," said William Gale, an economist at the Brookings Institution, the research organization. "If we could control people's behaviors that well, we wouldn't be in this situation in the first place."

HEADLINE: The World: Missionaries; 
On a Frontier Of Danger

BYLINE:  By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: RIO DE JANEIRO 

 BODY:
AMERICAN missionaries working in Latin America have always had to contend with obstacles, starting with distrustful local churches and governments. But the death of a Baptist missionary and her infant daughter, shot down in Peru on April 20 after their plane was confused with that of drug traffickers, is a sobering reminder that even as the success of their evangelization effort has grown, so have the risks faced by the more than 10,000 American missionaries now in the region.
 Since they first began heading south in the mid-1800's, American missionaries have had to labor under the twin burdens of being foreigners and, for the most part, Protestant. In a region where the original Iberian conquerors themselves imposed Catholicism by "the sword and the cross," it was probably inevitable that the Americans would end up being stigmatized as agents of cultural and political imperialism. 
 Nonetheless, in recent years, Protestantism, especially of the evangelical variety, has made remarkable gains in countries ranging from Guatemala to Brazil, most notably in cities. As a result, according to David Stoll, the author of "Is Latin America Turning Protestant?: The Politics of Evangelical Growth," "the frontier of the faith is now out in rural areas" like the Amazon, where Veronica and James Bowers were living when their plane was attacked.
But missionary organizations that choose to work in such remote areas, where the state's presence is weak and the population is largely Indian, have been regarded with a particularly suspicious eye. In country after country, groups like the New Tribes Mission and the Summer Institute of Linguistics, a branch of the Wycliffe Bible Translators, have been accused of spying for the C.I.A. or of clandestinely prospecting for gold, diamonds, emeralds or uranium.
"We are fully aware that we have been accused of being duplicitous," said David Oltrogge, director of Latin American Initiatives for the Wycliffe Bible Translators, which has compiled dictionaries in more than 300 American Indian languages. "But our motivation is to translate the Bible into as many languages as we can."
On the other hand, because American missionary groups have modern organizational and record-keeping skills, the United States Agency for International Development sometimes awards contracts to their local affiliates, and a congressional investigation in the 1970's found that many missionaries returning to the United States were debriefed by the C.I.A.
In recent years, "those criticisms have dwindled to insignificance compared to the problem of drug lords and paramilitaries killing people who will not cooperate with them," said Dr. Stoll, a professor of anthropology at Middlebury College. But the overall level of danger faced by missionaries -- including kidnapping for ransom -- has grown, especially in Peru's neighbor Colombia, where some organizations are pulling back from "portions of the country that it is ridiculous even to think of entering," as Dr. Oltrogge put it. 
Many American missionary groups working in Latin America began their activities in Asia or Africa, where they served as bearers of Western values. When they first arrived in Latin America, they quickly discovered they were on different terrain: this region is part of the West, and Christianity is the native religion.
Still, until recently the relationship between Protestant missionaries and the local Catholic hierarchy was largely one of mutual hostility and suspicion. 
"Even today, the majority of Protestant American missionaries are more likely to be conservative evangelicals than from the so-called mainline denominations," said Robert T. Coote of the Overseas Ministries Study Center. "We grew up thinking Catholicism couldn't possibly be right, that it was harmful even."
During the 1970's and into the 1980's, military regimes were "interested in ways of clipping the wings of the Catholic Church," as Ralph della Cava of Columbia University puts it, and in Protestantism they thought they had found an ideal vehicle. Like religious minorities in other parts of the world, Protestants sought to cooperate with whatever government was in power -- rendering unto Caesar the things that are Caesar's.
For Roman Catholic missionaries from the United States, many of them drawn to Latin America by the theology of liberation, the situation was almost exactly the opposite. The 1980 rape and murder of three American nuns and a lay worker by soldiers in El Salvador was only the most grotesque example of American missionaries suffering the same repression as the liberals among their Latin American brethren.
Today, the byword for American missionaries in the region appears to be "cultural sensitivity," said Mr. Coote. Particularly among Protestants, "Christian anthropology" has developed, aimed at making proselytizing less intrusive -- and more effective.

HEADLINE: Reckonings; 
The Real Wolf

BYLINE:  By PAUL KRUGMAN 

 BODY:
Recently I received a letter from an economist I respect, chiding me for my "Naderite" columns on the California energy crisis. He just didn't believe that market manipulation by power companies could possibly be an important issue; it sounded too much to him like the sort of thing one hears from knee-jerk leftists, who blame greedy capitalists for every problem, be it third-world poverty or high apartment rents. The left has cried "Wolf!" so many times that sensible people have learned to discount such claims.
 But now a bona fide wolf has arrived, whose predatory behavior is doing terrible damage to our most populous state -- and nobody will believe it. 
 True, California would be heading for a summer of power shortages even if it had never deregulated. And even if there was workable competition in the wholesale electricity market, prices in that market would spike during periods of peak demand, transferring billions of dollars from either taxpayers or consumers to the generators.
But the evidence is now overwhelming that there isn't workable competition in California's power market, and that the actions of generators "gaming the system" have greatly magnified the crisis. The key fact is that California has somehow remained in a state of more or less continuous power shortage and very high wholesale prices regardless of the level of demand. A rash of outages has kept the electricity market conveniently -- and very profitably -- short of supply even during periods of low demand, when there ought to be lots of excess capacity. 
As Frank Wolak, the Stanford economist who also advises the state's power grid, has pointed out, an outage at a power plant is a lot like an employee calling in sick. You can't tell directly whether he is really sick or has chosen to take the day off for other reasons, but you can look for circumstantial evidence. And such evidence has convinced Mr. Wolak that "generators use forced outages strategically to withhold capacity from the market" -- a view shared by a growing number of other researchers.
Which brings us to the latest move by the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission. On Wednesday, the commission apparently decided to offer California some relief, and put new price caps in place on the California electricity market. I say "apparently" because the more you look at the plan the less likely it seems to be any help at all. Indeed, the measure was passed on a 2-to-1 vote, with William Massey -- the one commissioner who has been sympathetic to calls for price controls -- voting against it on the grounds that it would be ineffectual.
What's wrong with FERC's plan? First, it caps prices only in emergency conditions -- ignoring the fact that electricity prices have stayed at hard-to-explain levels even when there is no emergency. In effect, the plan is laid out as if the electricity market were really competitive, in spite of all the evidence that it is not.
Second, even those emergency price caps are full of loopholes, offering extensive opportunities for what Mr. Wolak calls "megawatt laundering" -- selling power to affiliated companies that for one reason or another are exempted from the price controls (for example, the controls do not apply to "imports" from neighboring states), then selling it back into the California market. Severin Borenstein of the University of California Energy Institute adds that because the allowed price depends on the cost of generation at the least efficient plant, generators will have a clear incentive to produce inefficiently: "I predict we will find some plants we never heard of before that are suddenly operating again, and they will be pretty inefficient."
The general verdict seems to be that this is not a serious plan. There are serious proposals to mitigate the crisis out there -- indeed, last fall Mr. Wolak submitted a proposal that was well received by other experts -- but FERC has ignored all of them.
The charitable interpretation is that FERC still doesn't get it, that it just can't bring itself to believe that this time the wolf is real. The uncharitable interpretation is that last week's action was meant to fail. The Medley Report, an online newsletter, calls the FERC plan "a grand exercise in posturing without substance . . . a very clever temporary move by the Bush administration to deflect any political fallout" from the looming disaster.
Whatever the explanation, the plain fact is that FERC and the administration have yet to offer California any significant relief.

HEADLINE: DataBank; 
Economic Growth Revives Momentum

BYLINE:  By DYLAN LOEB McCLAIN 

 BODY:
After two of the best weeks for stocks in months, investors took some profits last Monday, but plunged back in by week's end when a report on economic growth in the first quarter showed surprising strength. Investors were also buoyed by good news on consumer spending as home sales in March grew at their fastest pace ever. 
 Technology stocks were the one sector that continued to show weakness as more job cuts were announced by industry stalwarts like JDS Uniphase and Compaq Computer. In another sign that some big companies are still worried about their prospects, Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing announced a pre-emptive job cut, despite solid earnings. 
 Blue chips did the best, with the Dow Jones industrials climbing to 10,810.05, up 230.20 points, or 2.2 percent. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index was also up, finishing at 1,253.05, up 10.07, or 0.8 percent. Only the Nasdaq fell, ending at 2,075.68, down 87.73, or 4.1 percent. 
 Treasury prices lost ground on fears that the better-than-expected economic growth makes it less likely that the Federal Reserve will cut interest rates further. The yield on the 10-year Treasury rose to 5.32 percent, from 5.29 percent a week earlier. DYLAN LOEB McCLAIN

HEADLINE: BUSINESS: DIARY; 
In a Default Record, A California Flavor

BYLINE:  By Rick Gladstone 

 BODY:
Here is a statistic that at first glance should stimulate the salivary glands of bankruptcy lawyers and buyers of failed businesses: In the first quarter of 2001, the Standard & Poor's credit rating agency says, 48 companies worldwide defaulted on $37 billion of debt, making it the worst quarter for defaults -- or, depending on how you look at it, the best. Of the defaulters, 41 were American companies. 
 To put that first-quarter total in some perspective, it equals 87 percent of the $42.3 billion in corporate defaults for all of 2000, which was an annual record. Based on first-quarter trends, the 2001 default ratio, a measure of corporate financial weakness that divides defaults by the number of corporate debt issuers, would be the worst since the 1991 recession year. 
 Behind the red ink, however, were some special circumstances. More than one-third of the total default amount came from Pacific Gas and Electric and Southern California Edison, which have amassed enormous debt because of California's energy deregulation crisis. Rick Gladstone

HEADLINE: LIVING ON WELFARE: A CLOCK IS TICKING 

BYLINE:  By SOMINI SENGUPTA 

DATELINE: WALTON, N.Y. 

 BODY:
When welfare changes came to this hamlet in the Catskill Mountains, the clock began ticking on Jennifer Mosher's lifetime entitlement to a welfare check.
 Since then, Ms. Mosher, 23, a single mother of two, has learned to drive. She has inherited a clunker of a car, held down a part-time job for an unprecedented four months ringing up groceries at a supermarket 25 miles away. She has thanked the stars for a baby sitter who agreed to match her erratic work schedule, a 10 a.m. shift one day, a 2:30 p.m. shift another. 
 But even as she has met the requirements of the government's latest welfare laws, the clock has kept ticking: Ms. Mosher does not make enough to get off welfare. If nothing changes, she will reach her five-year lifetime limit on cash benefits by the end of this year. Once that happens, state officials have said, she will receive vouchers to help pay the rent and buy food, though no one seems to know exactly how that will work. 
In Ms. Mosher's story lies the tale of what it is to be poor in rural America, where the usual hurdles to self-sufficiency are complicated by the difficulty of finding work and the difficulty of getting to work. A lot of poor women here in Delaware County grew up without learning how to drive, much less having a car of their own.
Delaware County has a larger story to tell. Since new rules signed by President Bill Clinton went into effect at the end of 1996, welfare rolls have plummeted here and all over the state. Unlike other counties, Delaware is able today to offer a rare glimpse into life after welfare. This year the county's leading social services group, Delaware Opportunities, released a survey -- thought to be the only countywide study in New York State -- of people leaving the welfare rolls. 
The study found, for instance, that after leaving welfare, families in the county continued to fall behind on rent. They were just as likely to have their lights shut off, almost as likely to live without a phone and more likely to have no food in the house. After welfare, survey respondents said, they were more likely to turn to a food bank, a church or friends. Or, they said, they simply "ate less."
The study found that poverty remained a familiar companion among those who left the rolls. The median income of those who left welfare in 2000 was about $11,000, and 70 percent remained below the poverty line, a little over $14,000 for a family of three. Those who had left welfare a year earlier were slightly better off: 45 percent lived in poverty. Only half of those who had left the rolls were working at the time of the survey. Around 30 percent depended on disability benefits. Those who found work were distinguished in one important way: the vast majority of them had a car.
National studies mirror many of these findings. Most of those who have left the welfare rolls have found work, and single mothers in particular have had a marked increase in income. But the research has also turned up a picture of hardship. A recent study by the Urban Institute, a liberal research group, of 40,000 families in 13 states found that about a third of the women who left welfare in 1997 said they had to reduce the size of meals or skip them completely because they did not have enough food.
Delaware County is a 1,460-square-mile terrain of rolling hills and frigid serpentine streams. Most of its 47,000 residents live in small villages separated from one another by miles of narrow, winding roads. It is the fourth-poorest county in the state, its median family income hovering around $24,000. There are no big factories near here, and the few jobs, at supermarkets, two state university campuses and fast-food restaurants, are sprinkled throughout the county. The economy that boomed elsewhere did little more than whimper here.
But since 1996, Delaware County's welfare rolls dropped by 66 percent. Rural counties all over the state saw their caseloads drop by more than 50 percent, and the statewide average was around 48 percent. 
Still, 71,000 families remain on welfare throughout the state and are running up against the time limit at the end of this year. More than a fourth of them are working, just like Ms. Mosher.
Jennifer Mosher's trajectory is not all that unusual. She was raised by a doting grandmother in Margaretville, on the county's southern edge. She dropped out of school in the ninth grade. She was pregnant at 18. She married at 19, to a man who she said made all the decisions at home, from buying groceries to dressing the children. 
When he left her, Ms. Mosher was utterly lost. She recalls the first time she went grocery shopping by herself. "I just stood there," she recalled. "I didn't know what to buy."
The next time, her grandmother went along, showing her what to buy, counseling her on bargains. 
Life on her own has been overwhelming. She says she was fired from her first job, at a local hospital, because the baby sitter showed up only sporadically.
She says she was evicted from the apartment she lived in because she let her sister move in with her boyfriend in tow.
Welfare reform has shined a harsh light on how stuck welfare mothers can be in a place like this. Job options are few: a dollar store, a McDonald's, a market and not much else. The supermarket where Ms. Mosher works is 25 miles away, in Oneonta.
There are no reliable bus lines in the Catskills. At one time, women like Ms. Mosher could hitch a ride every now and then to take the kids to the doctor or to visit the welfare office in Delhi, the county seat. The work requirements of the current welfare law have rendered useless that way of life.
While the federal welfare law allows state and local governments to use federal welfare money to help people get to and from work, transportation policy is hardly uniform. A recent study by the Greater Upstate Law Project, a statewide advocacy group, found that in some counties welfare recipients were stripped of benefits because they could not get to their jobs. In others, those on welfare were expected to walk up to four miles each way before the county would help pay for transportation. 
In this respect, Delaware County has proven to be especially creative. A van service, run by Delaware Opportunities, ferries people back and forth from job interviews and work assignments. Old, rotting cars are fixed up and sold at cut-rate prices.
That's how Ms. Mosher got her charcoal 1990 Ford Tempo. The car, she readily admits, has been her greatest blessing but also her biggest headache. She pays Delaware Opportunities $50 a month in car payments. Gas and her $130-a-month car insurance payment eat a hole in her wallet. Every other week, it seems, she has to take the car to the garage. Ball bearings need to be fixed. One front tire is balding precariously. Just talking about it one recent morning made her crave a cigarette.
Her paychecks vary -- $80 on a bad week, when she is scheduled to work barely 14 hours, $140 on a good one. The county still sends $300 in food stamps every month, the same as before she got a job, but only $128 a month in cash assistance, less than a third of what she got before she began working. The county pays for the baby sitter, and Ms. Mosher is responsible for a fourth of her rent, $96 a month. Welfare reform was supposed to make it easier for single mothers to collect child support. That has not happened yet for Ms. Mosher.
What has happened is that she has gotten a bank account, a new apartment and friends to commiserate with at the supermarket. She speaks the language of self-sufficiency. "If something has to be paid," she declares, "I pay it."
But here in Ms. Mosher's home, a gray clapboard apartment building pressed hard against the hills, self-sufficiency seems just this side of chaos. Dishes are piled in the sink. The living room carpet is awash in flecks of Doritos, dolls, a red fire truck and bright articles of children's clothes.
A social worker from Delaware Opportunities, Peg Gancio, sits on the floor, gently asking Ms. Mosher to estimate how much she spends on gasoline every week. Ms. Mosher sits on the sofa, hunched over a calculator. Her children, Kristen, 4, and Archie, 2, climb on top of her as if she were a jungle gym. The question of gas money is confounding. She has other things on her mind. A bunch of bills are due in a matter of days, and it's Kristen's birthday. Ms. Mosher still cannot afford a phone.
"Do you want me to stop by tomorrow and help you do your budget?" Ms. Gancio asks.
Ms. Mosher looks at the carpet. "Yes," she murmurs. "I don't know how." In fact, she admits, there was a time she didn't know how she would manage life on her own. When she dropped out of school, she figured she would stay with her grandmother. When she married, she figured the same of her husband. 
When the new welfare law was first explained to her some years ago, she figured she had time. When she did everything the new law asked of her, she figured she was making progress.
And yet, the clock keeps ticking. She says she has no idea what she will do if she is still here by December, working part time at the supermarket. She talks of maybe getting out of this tiny village, so far from anything. "I sit there trying to imagine: I'm still sitting in Walton, and they cut me off, and my job is giving me only 14 hours," she says. "I think about it all the time."
