PERSON: Charles Schreiner III 

HEADLINE: Charles Schreiner III, 74, Dies; Colorful Texas Rancher Fought to Save Longhorn 

BYLINE:  By DOUGLAS MARTIN 

 BODY:
Charles Schreiner III, who as he led a fight to preserve the legendary Texas longhorn breed became a Lone Star State symbol himself, died of congestive heart failure on April 22 at a San Antonio hospital.
 He was 74 and lived in Mountain Home, Tex. 
 Known by the nickname Three, he had perhaps his most colorful moment on the evening of April 13, 1980, when he celebrated the centennial of the day his grandfather (a Texas ranger, naturally) acquired the sprawling Y.O. Ranch by riding Ranger, his giant pet longhorn steer with four-foot horns, into the great hall of his ranch's main lodge.
"We're trying to preserve a way of life," Mr. Schreiner explained. 
Indeed, his life might be seen as an inspired scramble to sustain the Texas of legend. He started the Texas Longhorn Breeders' Association of America in 1964 to preserve the lean and lanky cattle. Their numbers had declined to 1,000 or fewer from the tens of millions in the late 19th century, when most of them ran wild. His namesake had herded more than 300,000 longhorn steers to Dodge City, Kan., over his life. 
The association defined and regulated the breed for the first time and undertook many educational and promotional campaigns. The number of longhorns has since grown to 250,000, and Mr. Schreiner's own tiny herd expanded to more than 1,000, making it one of the nation's largest.
The most important part of the effort was a breeders' registry to ensure pedigrees. "That really preserved the breed," said Alan Sparger III, a rancher in Comfort, Tex.
 Paraphrasing a country song, Mr. Sparger said of Mr. Schreiner, "He was longhorns when longhorns weren't cool." 
Mr. Schreiner did not stop with longhorns. He also led a movement to raise exotic animals from Africa and Asia on Texas ranches and then charge hunters to shoot them. The Y.O. Ranch, about 100 miles northwest of San Antonio in the Texas Hill Country, says it has the largest menagerie of what came to be called Texotics, around 50 species. About 35, including giraffes and zebras, cannot be hunted legally, but form the core of what has become a popular and bloodless tourist attraction.
Mr. Schreiner set up a youth camp, restaurant and dude ranch and became an expert at staging spectacles to attract visitors and attention. One of the most famous was a day when guests could shoot machine guns. After cocktails and dinner, they would riddle a car containing gasoline with tracer bullets until it exploded.
This Memorial Day weekend, the 26th annual Y.O. Ranch Longhorn Trail Drive will be held. The Y.O. came from the brand of the first longhorns bought by Mr. Schreiner's grandfather. 
The celebration will include an 1880's costume party. Participation costs $200 a person. (Rental of a horse is $150 extra, but participants are welcome to bring their own.)
Yet another strategy to preserve most of the family spread was to sell small parcels, 100 acres or so, as ranch shares. Owners were entitled to roam the entire 50,000 acres and pretend it was theirs.
"No matter what the economy does, you can count on a Texan for two things," Mr. Schreiner said. "He wants to own a ranch, and he's proud of being a Texan. It's bred into us."
All the plans meant that though the ranch shrank from 70,000 acres when Mr. Schreiner inherited it in 1949 to less than 50,000 acres, it remained in the family. The rocky, hardscrabble cactus patch with nary a drop of oil survived on the backs of longhorns and all manner of tourists.
"He was very creative," said Don L. King, executive director of the longhorn association.
Mr. Schreiner was an only child, born in San Antonio on Jan. 6, 1927. He was educated in San Antonio private schools and earned a business degree from the University of Texas. His father died when Mr. Schreiner was 6, and he was educated in ranch affairs by his mother, Myrtle, and the ranch hands he adored.
Longhorns had almost died out as a result of being bred with plumper breeds. By 1940, the Y.O. Ranch had none, and the glory days of Mr. Schreiner's grandfather, who fought with the Confederates in the Civil War before becoming a Texas ranger, were a distant memory. 
In 1957, Mr. Schreiner set out to assemble a new longhorn herd.
"He'd find one here and one there and a handful over there," his son Walter said. "He traveled tens of thousands of miles just looking for them. A lot of dead ends."
In a 1987 interview with The San Antonio Express-News, Mr. Schreiner said: "Without this breed, there wouldn't have been a Y.O. It just wasn't right for them to become extinct."
Others were doing the same thing, including an Oregon longhorn owner who was considering setting up an association and breeding registry. Mr. Schreiner was of the opinion that both belonged in Texas, and in 1964 he filed a certificate of incorporation. He became the first president.
His first task was hiring Claude Shrader, who had worked at a federal refuge for longhorns in Oklahoma, established in the 1920's. He was renowned for being able to look at a cow years after having seen it as a calf and remember its pedigree. Mr. Shrader traveled the country inspecting would-be Texas longhorns, at $2 a head.
In 1965, Mr. Schreiner hit upon the idea of staging a re-enactment of a trail drive from San Antonio to Dodge City to observe the 100th anniversary of the first trail drive after the Civil War. The group stopped at President Lyndon B. Johnson's ranch on the way, and made national news.
Since then, longhorn breeding has become so widespread and specialized that cattle are bred for characteristics like horn length and muscle mass. Because the longhorn produces lean, grain-fed meat, it has become popular with people conscious of their diet.
Mr. Schreiner's wife, Audrey, a University of Texas cheerleader when he met her, died in 1988. One son, Louis, died of a heart attack on April 15. In addition to his son Walter, of Mountain Home, Mr. Schreiner is survived by his sons Charles IV, of Salado, Tex., and Gus, also of Mountain Home; and nine grandchildren.
Mr. Schreiner habitually wore a beat-up cowboy hat and collected antique Colt pistols. In 1986, Sports Illustrated described him as a "stocky, round-faced man with a grizzled mustache who looks like a hammered-down Hemingway."
Mr. King of the association recalled him as a tough guy and then gave him the ultimate Texas compliment. "He had an eye for cattle," Mr. King said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Charles Schreiner III at a party in 1980 to celebrate the 100th anniversary of the founding of the Y.O. Ranch. (Wendy Watriss)

HEADLINE: PRO FOOTBALL; 
Giants Praise a Little-Known Kicker

BYLINE:  By BILL PENNINGTON 

DATELINE: EAST RUTHERFORD, N.J., April 28 

 BODY:
An Eagle Scout, the son of an engineer and a bond trader, John Markham considered attending a service academy but ultimately chose Vanderbilt University in Nashville because it was close to his Tennessee home and was valued for its academics.
 Markham had been an all-state place-kicker in high school, but not an all-American and not actively recruited by major college football powers. He would play four years of football at Vanderbilt, ending a successful career as a team captain. But when his senior season was over last fall, he was not invited to any of the postseason all-star games, nor to the National Football League scouting combine. No N.F.L. team asked him to come for a visit or a workout. 
 So on the second day of the N.F.L. draft last weekend, Markham, who will graduate next month with a degree in economics, was sitting in his dormitory with his roommate. They were casually watching the N.F.L. draft unfold. Markham figured he would probably end up trying out for a team in training camp.
He also knew that his father, Mark, had suggested getting an M.B.A.
"To say that I expected to be drafted, to see my name on the television screen, that wouldn't be right," Markham said Friday. "I figured I would end up with some team eventually. You could say I was surprised, pleasantly surprised, to find out I was drafted."
He was the Giants' fifth-round draft choice, and the second kicker selected. Billy Gramatica, brother of the Pro Bowl kicker Martin Gramatica, had gone to the Arizona Cardinals one round earlier. Markham may not have been listed in any of the exhaustive publications that handicap the draft -- even when the list of kickers went 20 deep -- but he was one of only three kickers taken.
And now Markham, who looks a little like a teenage Ron Howard, is the immediate favorite to win the Giants' starting place-kicker's job. Teams do not expend fifth-round draft picks on kickers or punters they expect to cut.
Last year's kicker, Brad Daluiso, is a 33-year-old free agent, and the team is not pursuing him. Jaret Holmes was on the team's practice squad for most of last year and considered the heir apparent to Daluiso -- before the Giants dropped the Markham bombshell. And while the Giants have said Markham and Holmes will compete for the starting job at training camp, the praise from the coaching staff and front office for Markham has been bountiful.
Giants General Manager Ernie Accorsi conceded that Holmes had an equal chance to make the team, but added, "If he wins the job, he will have beat out a heck of a kicker."
How can the Giants have such confidence in a place-kicker that few outside Nashville had heard of until last weekend?
"There have always been great kickers who aren't celebrated," Accorsi said. "The answer is that Jan Stenerud went to Montana on a ski scholarship."
Stenerud, who learned to kick an American football after enrolling at Montana State, is the only pure kicker in the Pro Football Hall of Fame.
"Our scouts and coaches did the work on him, and they had him as our highest-rated kicker," Accorsi said of Markham, who made 14 of 18 field goals last year at Vanderbilt. "I'm going to listen to them, not anybody else. We liked Markham's hang time on kickoffs, and he was incredibly consistent. He did not make a huge number of kicks from 50 yards and beyond, but I'm not sure at Vanderbilt that he got to try that many long kicks."
As for Holmes, who had a good season in practice as Daluiso's understudy, Accorsi said: "Look, we're doing this for a living. A guy has to have a little more history before we just hand him the job."
Holmes had two career N.F.L. field-goal attempts and made both.
Markham, meanwhile, celebrated his 22nd birthday Friday at the Giants' rookie minicamp. He arrived well aware that his selection puzzled many, especially reporters who cover the team.
"I understand that many people wondered," he said. "But everybody has a different take on kickers. Coaches' judgments aren't going to be the same. I don't mind taking the mind-set that I'm the underdog. I can get my kickoffs inside the 5-yard line with good hang time, and I've been extremely consistent inside 40 yards with my field goals."
Markham seemed unruffled by the large group of reporters he attracted Friday -- and by the consistent line of questioning that at least implied he was a confounding, mystery draft pick by the Giants. Markham was engaging, pleasant and winsome, adding at one point with a modest smile, "Back home, a long time ago, I was told to always read the sports pages like you read the funny pages."

GRAPHIC: Photo: John Markham (19) converted 14 of 18 field-goal attempts for Vanderbilt last season. He has a chance to win a starting job with the Giants. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Rich Nations Consider Fund Of Billions to Fight AIDS 

BYLINE:  By JOSEPH KAHN

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28 

 BODY:
The world's leading industrial nations are considering beginning a multibillion dollar global program to fight AIDS and other infectious diseases, but the United States has warned that such an effort would probably fail unless it is carefully tailored to the difficult conditions in countries where such diseases are most prevalent.
 The push to commit new aid money was a significant topic of discussion at the meeting of Group of 7 finance ministers in Washington today. Some financial officials, especially those from European countries, are pushing the United States and other wealthy nations to devote unprecedented amounts of money to finance a global assault on AIDS, malaria and tuberculosis, especially in Africa. 
 The gathering of the top economic and financial leaders of the United States, Canada, Germany, France, Britain, Italy and Japan took place on the sidelines of the spring meetings of the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, the two leading global lending institutions. 
Officials of the World Bank and the United Nations, both of which have devoted hundreds of millions of dollars to fight AIDS and other diseases, have argued that existing financing is woefully inadequate.
James D. Wolfensohn, president of the World Bank, says the amount necessary to start an effective AIDS prevention and treatment program in Africa is $3 billion to $4 billion a year. That is 10 times the amount the developed world provides today.
The Bush administration has not ruled out participation in such an effort, which European ministers hope to begin when the leaders of the Group of 7 nations have their main annual summit meeting in Genoa, Italy, in July. But a senior Treasury Department official said the United States had raised several red flags.
The official said Africa lacked the basic medical and physical infrastructure that would make it possible to deploy effectively the complex cocktail of drugs to fight AIDS. He said Africans lacked a requisite "concept of time," implying that they would not benefit from drugs that must be administered on tight time schedules.
"This kind of thing should not be undertaken to salve the consciences of those who are well intentioned," the Treasury official said after the Group of 7 meeting this evening. "There has to be understanding of how to bring about meaningful change in the lives of real people."
The official said the final decision about whether the United States will take part rested with President Bush. He said no one should "jump to any conclusion" about whether the United States would join the effort.
The discussion about diseases came as the financial leaders discussed the health of their own economies. All the major industrial economies are growing "below their potential," to use the official euphemism.
The International Monetary Fund warned this week that global growth would slump to 3.2 percent this year, compared with the estimate of 4.8 percent it made in late 2000. The question is what, if anything, the finance ministers can agree to do about it.
The leaders of the International Monetary Fund have made the loudest calls for aggressive action by world leaders to prompt fresh growth by slashing interest rates, thus easing the availability of credit to encourage consumer spending and business investment. The United States has also pushed Japan and European nations to undertake what are known as "structural reforms," or changes to labor laws and investment barriers that are thought to stifle productivity and growth.
But Treasury Secretary Paul H. O'Neill has sought to strike a more conciliatory tone when meeting with his counterparts than his predecessor, Lawrence H. Summers, who was often accused of lecturing allies.
The communique issued at the end of the session struck an optimistic tone and avoided what economists see as the biggest obstacle to world growth: the conservative interest rate policy of the new European Central Bank.
"Although global growth has slowed over the past year, the foundations for economic expansion are sound," the joint statement from the ministers said. It made no mention of European interest rates, though it did call for Japan to maintain "ample liquidity" in order to combat falling prices in that country.
Mr. O'Neill made the case that the United States was doing its part to help revive global growth.
The Federal Reserve has cut interest rates four times since January. Mr. Bush has also made a $1.6 billion tax cut the centerpiece of his economic plan, while agreeing to speed up some tax relief to act as a near-term stimulus to consumer spending.
"In the U.S.," Mr. O'Neill said. "we're on track to make good on the idea that the U.S. can be a strong engine of growth for the world economy, and it's my hope and expectation that others will do the same."

HEADLINE: Preparing to Grieve For a Catholic School 

BYLINE:  By DAVID M. HERSZENHORN 

 BODY:
For Corey Patrick, a 31-year-old city bus driver who lives in Bedford-Stuyvesant, Brooklyn, sending his son to All Saints School in Harlem is very convenient. The school, on East 130th Street in Manhattan, is just minutes from the bus depot at 146th Street and Lenox Avenue, where his shift ends at 2 p.m.
 Sitting in his blue sport utility vehicle outside the school on a recent afternoon, Mr. Patrick was the portrait of a modern New York dad, waiting for the clock to strike three and for his son, Corey Jr., to come bounding out at the end of another day of fifth grade. 
 All Saints, with 224 students from prekindergarten through eighth grade, is one of six financially failing parish schools facing possible closure by the Archdiocese of New York because of budget cuts. Cardinal Edward M. Egan and archdiocesan officials have reviewed business plans from the six schools and are expected to announce a decision Tuesday, the archdiocesan spokesman, Joseph Zwilling, said yesterday. 
Most parents interviewed at All Saints last week said that they had no idea what they would do if the school closed. 
Waiting for their children in the afternoon sun, they explained how All Saints fit so well into their hectic working class lives. Many parents said that although they were not Catholic, they preferred a Catholic school to overcrowded, under-performing public schools. 
For David Fletcher of the South Bronx, who was parked just ahead of Mr. Patrick, All Saints' tuition of $1,850 a year was an affordable option for his daughter, Tracie, a first grader. "I checked the tuition some of the schools had, and this was one that I could pay for," he said. 
For Patricia Garvin, who lives in White Plains and works as a teacher's assistant at Public School 72 on East 104th Street, having her 4-year-old daughter, Damon'e, in prekindergarten at All Saints offers peace of mind. "She has to be near where I am," Ms. Garvin said. "Just in case of emergencies." 
And for Daphne Ballard, who lives near the school in a neighborhood slowly rebounding from decades of social and economic distress, seeing her daughters Courtney, 12, and Zoe, 7, grow up at All Saints has been a personal triumph. She smiles proudly when she thinks about Courtney graduating next year. 
"I wanted her to have some constancy, because I was moved around a lot when I was a kid," Ms. Ballard said. "So this is an achievement for me to have her be able to say she went to one school." 
The six schools, three in Manhattan including All Saints, and one each in Rockland, Dutchess and Ulster Counties, all have low enrollment and have operated at a deficit for years. The archdiocese says it can no longer afford to fill the gaps. 
The parish pastor, the Rev. Theodore C. Parker, said that All Saints' income this year would be $797,800, including rent from leasing its top two floors to a Jesuit program for boys. But the school's total expenses, including funds for extra art, music, computers and Spanish classes, are expected to be $889,400, Father Parker said, leaving a $91,600 deficit. 
A decision to raise tuition next year to $2,000 from $1,850 was made a few weeks before the archdiocese announced in late March that All Saints was on the list of schools marked for possible closing. 
To many working poor parents or those who have more than one child in the school, it is not a small increase. Still, All Saints remains one of the most affordable Catholic schools in New York.
"A lot of the parents are working two jobs, and we have single parents," said the principal, Anthony Georges. "They are willing to sacrifice to see that their children get a good education." 
With just four weeks to prepare a business plan, Father Parker and Mr. Georges stepped up efforts to create an advisory board to help the school with fund-raising. 
Teachers telephoned parents and implored them to register early for next year. The Children's Storefront School, a free private school on East 129th Street, called the parents of children on its waiting list to urge them to consider All Saints. And clergy at nearby churches that do not operate schools urged parishioners to send their children to the school. 
Fewer than 80 students usually register in April, Mr. Georges said, but this year 175 have already signed up. Father Parker predicted that enrollment would rise by about 25 to 250 and that the deficit would be erased.
"With that number, we are certain we will not have to ask the archdiocese for money," Father Parker said. "We think that we'll get through this." 
Closing All Saints is unthinkable, many parents said. A tour of the school explains why. Classrooms are large, comfortable and immaculate. Hallways are quiet. Students wear maroon and gray uniforms, with neckties for the boys. 
All Saints, the parents said, is an integral part of lives balanced as delicately as a coin standing on edge. Ms. Garvin, the parent who works at a nearby school, may have to look for a job near her home in White Plains if All Saints closes so that her daughter can attend public school. Or, Ms. Garvin said, she could enroll her daughter in private school near her parents' home in the Bronx. But she would prefer to keep her daughter at All Saints. "I just like the smallness of the class size, and the community in this area is good," she said. 
Diane Hill, a medical assistant, enrolled her oldest son, Ben, 12, at All Saints for first grade when the family lived in Harlem. Now they live farther uptown, in Inwood, but Ben, now in sixth grade, remains at All Saints. His younger brother, Marquis, 3, is in prekindergarten.
Mrs. Hill said that she considered enrolling Ben in public school when he first started. "They were teaching in bathrooms," she said. "They were teaching in the gyms. There were trailers they were teaching in, and the public schools weren't safe." 
Mrs. Hill said she liked having a school near family members but that parents at the school also helped one another. "We all know each other and depend on each other, and we don't want to give that up." 
Mrs. Hill, pregnant with her third child, said she hoped the new baby could also go to All Saints. "I hope she can make it there," she said. "If they keep it open long enough."

HEADLINE: Tiny Island Turns Into a Symbol of Discontent 

BYLINE:  By ANDREW JACOBS

DATELINE: VIEQUES, P.R., April 28 

 BODY:
The politicians, celebrities and cameramen had turned in for the night, but Martina Rodriguez and her friends from the University of Puerto Rico in San Juan were in no mood to sleep. They talked politics, shared cold hamburgers and smoked cigarettes as a nearby contingent of police officers, looking bored and sleepy, stood behind barricades.
 It was nearly 2 this morning, and despite the exhaustion from a day of protests, Ms. Rodriguez, 19, said she wanted to stay up all night in case there was a skirmish with the police. "We've never felt anything like this before," said Ms. Rodriguez, who said she had been largely apolitical before Vieques became a rallying cry for millions of Puerto Ricans. "This is the Vietnam of my generation. We want to stop the mayhem. We want to make a difference." 
 The Navy's decision to resume bombing exercises here has galvanized the commonwealth of Puerto Rico, bringing together people of all ideologies and backgrounds who view the vast military firing range as a symbol of everything that is wrong with Puerto Rico's relationship with the United States.
More than 600 people have been arrested since April 1999, when a bombing accident killed a civilian security guard and sparked the rallies and vigils that have become a central theme for Puerto Rican politicians and the news media.
Gov. Sila M. Calderon, who was elected in November, the first woman to hold that post in Puerto Rico, made Vieques one of her top issues, promising to evict the Navy from Vieques. 
After years of feeling frustrated by a lack of popular support, Farrique Pesquera, an independence advocate, said he was heartened by the swelling anger among students, housewives, clergymen and Puerto Rican pop stars like Ricky Martin, Marc Anthony and Jose Feliciano. 
"People here have no self-esteem," Mr. Pesquera said, intentionally raising his voice so the police could hear. "They have been brainwashed to think that they can't survive without America, that all our air comes from the north. Struggles like this one will change that."
But the campaign to turn Vieques into a cause celebre is not universally admired among the 9,400 residents of this 33,000-acre island off the eastern tip of Puerto Rico. While many residents have painted "No Mas Bombas" -- no more bombs -- on their car windows, there is a quiet contingent that resents the protesters, the politicians and the reporters.
The most persistent protesters, the locals point out, are from the Big Island -- as Puerto Rico is called by Vieques residents -- intellectuals and ideologues who did not care a wisp about Vieques until recently. "These big city guys are just playing with us, using Vieques for their own political end," said Juan Morales, a 56-year-old handyman, as he sipped a beer at a roadside bodega. 
Some, like Tito Padro, a waiter, said the demonstrators missed the point when they shouted about the health problems wrought by 60 years of the Navy's dropping ordnance at Camp Garcia, the firing range on the eastern third of Vieques.
"The protesters and politicians say that the Navy has destroyed Vieques, but no one wants to help us deal with our real problems," he said. The real problems, he and others said, are poor roads, an overburdened sewage system, poor schools and an unemployment rate near 50 percent. "The bombing overshadows all our other problems," he said. 
And some who live off the island's fledgling tourist trade, many of them Americans, blame the protesters for scaring off business. Burr Vail, for one, said business at his hotel had been growing 20 percent a year until the battle over Vieques began two years ago. Now it is down 20 percent. "The Navy has been a poor neighbor, to be sure, but until recently we managed to co-exist," Mr. Vail said. 
Many restaurant and hotel owners say the effect of the bombing has been exaggerated, but a preliminary study by the Puerto Rican government has shown a high incidence of vibroacoustic disease, a rare disorder associated with loud noises. (The bombing range is nine miles from the nearest house.) 
Pete Baumgartner, 65, a South Carolina native who owns a gift shop here, described the sound of falling shells as distant thunder, not the heart-stopping explosions he has read about in the newspaper. The exercises, Mr. Baumgartner said, take place a few weeks each year, and before the protests began most tourists barely noticed they were sharing the island with the Navy.
Before the protests began, he said, many of Camp Garcia's beaches -- considered the best on Vieques -- were unofficially open to the public. These days even fishermen are forbidden from dropping their lobster traps in the restricted zone that extends a half mile from the coast. 
As the protesters held their all-night vigil, Mr. Baumgartner and his friends gathered at Bananas, a restaurant down the road, downing beers and grousing about the media circus, the Puerto Rican government's inattention to the island and the sense that life on Vieques will never be the same.
Rick Smith, 52, a security guard, said he had moved here from St. Thomas seven years ago to escape the commercialization that has transformed so many other Caribbean islands. "I love the fact that there's no Burger King, no Hiltons and no Hyatts," Mr. Smith said. 
About 4,000 tourists come here each year, but that number is expected to grow if the residents vote in favor of a referendum next November that would evict the Navy by 2003. Longtime residents like Carmen Perez, 47, believe the Navy's departure would put an end to the bad publicity and free thousands of acres controlled by the United States government. "We need progress," said Mrs. Perez, who has been unemployed for eight years. "We need more tourists."
But many tourists who take the 20-minute flight from San Juan say large-scale development would be a mistake. Paul Smith, 37, an audio technician from New York, said he found the Navy's presence here insulting but admitted that he liked the unintended result."We wanted to find a place that wasn't superdeveloped, where there isn't a casino and where music isn't piped into the street," he said. "I'm sure it must be awful to live with all that bombing, but I have to admit that if it weren't for the U.S. military, this place would have been ruined long ago."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Protesting the Navy's plan to resume bombing exercises on Vieques, a man waved a Puerto Rican flag yesterday near the island's firing range. (Reuters) Maps of Puerto Rico highlighting Vieques.

HEADLINE: Loved Humanity, Hated People 

BYLINE:  By Sylvia Nasar; Sylvia Nasar, the James S. and John L. Knight professor at the Columbia University Graduate School of Journalism, is the author of "A Beautiful Mind," a biography of the mathematician John Nash.

 BODY:
 Bertrand Russell
The Ghost of Madness, 1921-1970.
By Ray Monk.
Illustrated. 574 pp. New York:
The Free Press. $40.
 Bertrand Russell, who was among the 20th century's most influential philosophers and public intellectuals, began his autobiography by declaring, "Three passions, simple but overwhelmingly strong, have governed my life: the longing for love, the search for knowledge and unbearable pity for the suffering of mankind." Thus the author of "Principia Mathematica" and champion of free love and world peace explained his multiple marriages and affairs, towering intellectual achievements and numerous public honors, including a Nobel. 
Some of his best friends saw him in a less flattering light. "A fervid egoist," Virginia Woolf noted dryly in her diary in 1921, shortly before Russell's 50th birthday -- the halfway mark of what would be a remarkably long life. "A fallen angel with Mephistophelean wit," remarked Beatrice Webb, founder of the London School of Economics, adding, "He may be successful as a litterateur; I doubt whether he will be of value as a thinker, and I am pretty well certain he will not attain happiness." In the second volume of his prodigiously researched and brilliantly narrated biography, "Bertrand Russell: The Ghost of Madness, 1921-1970," Ray Monk, a professor of philosophy at the University of Southampton in England, goes even farther than Russell's contemporaries in deconstructing the iconic sage with the halo of unruly white hair.
"His great quality was his unfairness" is the mildest of many barbs repeated by Monk to suggest that the philosopher's professed love for humanity disguised a hatred of most people. The charge of unfairness could apply equally to Monk himself. While accusing Russell of oversimplifying complex issues and caricaturing his opponents, Monk has written an account that reads more like an indictment than a life. Despite his superb grasp of Russell's philosophical work and confident command of the details of Russell's private life, Monk, the author of a sympathetic yet rigorous biography of Ludwig Wittgenstein, is far too convinced of Russell's guilt to do his protean subject justice. 
Surveying the eventful second half of the British philosopher's life, Monk maintains that instead of love and pity, "a deep-seated fear of madness and a quite colossal vanity" drove Russell "inexorably" toward "disaster." Not only did Russell waste his intellectual gifts by abandoning highbrow philosophy for hack journalism (and political grandstanding), Monk says, but he caused enough emotional carnage in his own home to make the House of Atreus look like an average dysfunctional family. According to Monk, "loving another was, for him, inconceivable." Russell's chief victims, he claims, were his son John and his granddaughters Sarah and Lucy -- all of whom suffered from the same devastating mental illness, schizophrenia, that afflicted some of Russell's older relatives.
Monk's thesis makes for a compelling story line, but it simply isn't credible -- even on the evidence he supplies. Whatever Russell's flaws -- arrogance, grandiosity, glibness, insensitivity and occasionally hilarious flakiness come to mind -- he was neither as trivial nor as monstrous as Monk would have us believe.
Take Monk's first complaint, that Russell churned out gobs of "second-rate journalism" instead of sticking to mathematics and logic. Wittgenstein once said of his former mentor's work, "Russell's books should be bound in two colors, those dealing with mathematical logic in red -- and all students of philosophy should read them; those dealing with ethics and politics in blue -- and no one should be allowed to read them." Monk couldn't agree more; "willful shallowness," "wild exaggerations," "crass oversimplifications," "superficiality" and "credulous nonsense" are just a few of his put-downs of Russell's writings of the 20's and 30's. But are newspaper columns meant to be anything but ephemeral?
As it happens, Russell turned to freelance writing and the lecture circuit to support his wife and children -- as well as to be able to stay home caring for his young son. And some of Russell's popular work, including "A History of Western Philosophy," was good enough to merit a Nobel Prize in Literature. This cuts no ice with Monk. 
Monk's cruelest allegation is that Russell's shortcomings as a parent destroyed his son and doomed the granddaughters he and his fourth wife, Edith, raised. One, Sarah, spent "much of her time . . . in psychiatric care," and the other, Lucy, immolated herself five years after Russell's death. Indeed, in a gesture that must be called terminally tacky, Monk uses Lucy's death to point an accusing finger at Russell, calling it, in the book's closing lines, "the final visitation of the ghosts that haunted Russell throughout his life." 
Monk's vivid rendering of this family tragedy masks the speciousness of his underlying argument. Back in the Freudian 50's and 60's, schizophrenia was widely thought to be a psychological condition for which awful parents were to blame. But that belief has long since been thoroughly discredited by research, and by the efficacy of drug therapy. Both show that schizophrenia is a brain disorder like Alzheimer's or Parkinson's rather than the product of a traumatic "family romance." Not a hint of this is to be found in Monk's volume -- a glaring omission for a decade-long research project. 
In the book's second chapter, Monk cautions readers that "of course, the links between cause and effect here are very difficult to determine, and one should be wary of attributing John's later misfortunes directly to his upbringing." But Monk exhibits no such wariness. Just a few lines down, he writes: "John did not grow up to be fearless, independent and free, as Russell's theory demanded that he should, but rather inhibited, withdrawn and anxious. It is only in retrospect, however, that we can say: 'No wonder.' "
After describing the descent of John, whose troubles began in elementary school, into full-blown schizophrenia at 33, Monk leaves no doubt as to the cause of his illness. Referring to a nightmare shared by Russell and his granddaughter Lucy, he writes, "What Russell had sensed was a guilty secret of madness in the family; what Lucy had sensed was a hidden murder." Monk goes on to speculate: "Might this not be a metaphor for the feeling that her beloved grandfather had himself a dreadful secret, namely that he had destroyed the life of his son?"
Once again, Russell's actions count for very little. That Russell and John's mother, Dora, founded a school largely for their children's benefit, that Russell stayed in a miserable marriage (when his wife had borne two children by another man) to spare his children the pain of divorce, or that Russell provided a home for his troubled son and the son's equally troubled wife for several years doesn't alter Monk's verdict that he was "cold towards those who loved him." He prefers to see Russell as the sole author of his own and his family's misery. "At 41 Queen's Road, therefore, Russell did not just write his 'Autobiography'; he recreated it." 
WITNESSING a parent's grief over a child's devastating illness inspires sympathy in most people. And most people have some sense of the terrible choices that parents face -- choices even bleaker in 1955, when no effective treatments existed. But apparently not Monk. Russell was surely wrong to relegate his son to his ex-wife's care and to refuse further contact with him. But was that so monstrous? He was, not without reason, afraid of his son and determined to shield his granddaughters. For Monk, however, there are no shades of gray. "Dora struggled to cope with John, who was never again able to work or look after himself, and Russell got on with the task of saving humanity," he writes. Monk could just as easily have said, "and Russell got on with the task of caring for his granddaughters" -- but that would have undermined the case he seems determined to make.
Unfortunately, Monk's dislike of Russell -- which he says is based largely on the fate of Russell's son and granddaughters -- and his seeming ignorance of the most basic facts about mental illness have skewed his judgment badly. Biographers who despise their subjects are evidently just as much at risk of getting the story wrong as those who worship blindly.

HEADLINE: Books in Brief: Fiction; 
The Best Revenge in Manhattan

BYLINE:  By Kera Bolonik 

 BODY:
The playwright Han Ong's assured first novel, FIXER CHAO (Farrar, Straus & Giroux, $25), introduces us to William Narciso Paulinha, a Filipino street hustler who is eager to get out of the life -- that is, until he meets Shem C, a marginal writer with a chip on his shoulder and a proposition William can't resist. Shem hires him to assume the role of "Master Chao," a fictitious feng shui practitioner, as a vengeful way to fleece and humiliate the inner circle of Bill Hood, an esteemed novelist of Philip Roth stature, whose successes illuminate Shem's many failures, both in his career and his romantic life. 
As Master Chao, William proves to be a quick study, swiftly rising in status from seedy bar denizen to an A-list guest among the upper echelons of Manhattan society. As he supposedly arranges their decadent homes to invite good fortune, William discreetly heeds Shem's instructions to ensure that important details were "off by just a few crucial degrees," paving the way for his clients' destruction. Roguish though he is, William is a keen observer, and his ever-
 increasing resentment toward these people -- many of whom, he thinks, are impostors like him, and have relentlessly used their loyal mentors and friends to ascend both socially and economically -- feels wholly justified in the context of this mordantly funny novel. "This hate was what made me get out of bed in the morning," he confesses, priding himself on having become "the man who in himself had incarnated every single millennial fear that they'd nurtured . . . so that they could finally face their guilty consciences." Kera Bolonik

SECTION: Section 5; Column 1; Travel Desk; Pg. 10

LENGTH: 2565 words

HEADLINE: WEEKEND GETAWAYS; 
Boston on the Run: Durer to Jim Dine

BYLINE:  By AMY WALDMAN; AMY WALDMAN is a reporter on the metropolitan staff of The Times.

 BODY:
I SPEND a great deal of time with words: reading them, writing them, watching their monotonous march across a computer screen. So when I was asked to spend a weekend in Boston looking at art, I felt my right brain stirring with excitement. 
 I realized, as I arrived, that I didn't really know Boston, since all my recent visits had been to friends in Cambridge. Fortunately, it is a remarkably compact city, easy to navigate on foot or by the MBTA public transportation system. I ended up walking everywhere on Saturday; on Sunday, friends shuttled me around in their car. 
 I wanted to mix major museums with lesser-known galleries, and did some research online using citysearch.com, which has a listing of museums and current exhibits. Once in Boston, I got the magazine Art New England and the current Gallery Guide from Art Now. 
Friday night, I went straight to the Museum of Fine Arts. The grande dame of art in Boston, it has one of the country's largest collections, ranging from Egyptian and Asian art to European masterpieces to modern photography. On Fridays, its West Wing stays open late, and offers M.F.A. Fridays -- drinks, music and socializing along with the art. 
On the way in, I walked under a striding Jonathan Borofsky figure; there were more of his site-specific sculptures soaring inside. I strolled through part of the museum's remarkable Asian art collection, passing an anatomically correct 17th-century Tibetan sculpture of Vajrabhairava and his consort. Intricately whittled of painted copper, it is designed to represent a symbolic coupling of anger and wisdom that is said to destroy the attachment to the ego and produce enlightenment. The sculpture turned out to be an appropriate keynote for what was to come -- consorting, that is, as opposed to enlightenment. 
M.F.A. Fridays, it turns out, is a more heated pickup scene than I've seen in most bars. I quickly adopted eye contact avoidance techniques honed in New York subways and focused on the art. The room had a selection of 16th- and 17th-century European painting -- El Greco, Tintoretto, Rubens, Velazquez and others -- that tended toward the dour, with scenes of the Crucifixion, martyred saints and the like. The backdrop gave a slightly absurd cast to the meeting and mingling going on below. And it spawned pickup lines like, "So is this room all about the life of Jesus Christ, or what?" "Or what," I said, and fled. 
Fortunately, even on Friday night, or especially on Friday night, the museum offers many spots of beauty and solitude, starting with John Singer Sargent's stately, patrician portraits. The diversity of its collection is staggering: you wander through a room of striking African masks, crudely carved but carefully designed to ward off communal disaster, and suddenly find yourself face to face with a simple, but luminous, Georgia O'Keeffe calla lily. I resolved to return to the rest of the museum during the weekend, but never had time. 
I met some friends for dinner at Lala Rokh, a tranquil Persian restaurant on Beacon Hill. Along with 14th-century calligraphy on the walls, the restaurant had a spring-themed Zoroastrianism display that included a bowl of beautifully, if obsessively, hand-painted eggs in elaborate designs. 
I went "home" to the Eliot Hotel, at the corner of Massachusetts and Commonwealth Avenues in the Back Bay. Commonwealth, wide and lined with gorgeous houses, is one of Boston's prettiest streets, and the location is perfect for exploring museums and galleries. The hotel itself is simple -- a lobby, a restaurant, 10 rooms and 85 suites -- but pretty. And my light, airy room was spacious and nicely furnished, with sheets so soft I could barely bring myself to leave them. 
But I did, the next morning, after eating breakfast at my hotel. When I was a child growing up in California, on a trip "back East," as we called it, I had gone with my family to the Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum. The memory had never left me: a pink courtyard surrounding a garden, with romantic balconies and ivy trailing down the walls. It was a place, I had thought then, I wanted to live, so I made it my first stop Saturday morning. It was about a 15-minute walk along the Fenway from my hotel. 
The museum has not changed (nor, apparently, have I -- I still want to live there). The stunning Venetian palazzo is built around a courtyard filled with sculptures and vivid flowering plants, rather than outward onto a canal. The museum has a particularly strong collection of Italian Renaissance paintings, but there is more. On the ground floor is Sargent's enormous, darkly magical "El Jaleo," depicting a Spanish dancer; Anders Zorn's shadowy painting "The Omnibus," depicting a modern trolley car filled with a mix of classes, stayed with me, as did an intense five-part altarpiece by Simone Martini. The museum also has Titian's "Rape of Europa."
The driving force behind this place, Isabella Stewart Gardner, was a turn-of-the-century woman who had extraordinary vision and the formidable will (and the fortune) to match it. She amassed an amazing collection of art, designed the building, then determined how her art would be displayed, instructing that nothing could be permanently changed after her death. Some things were, tragically, beyond her control: in 1990, thieves raided the museum and stole a Vermeer and three Rembrandts, among other works, none of which have been recovered. The museum has left the paintings' frames in place, as if they've simply been taken out for a spell. Gaping like empty eye sockets, they cast a pall over the Dutch room. 
So it was restorative to hear, in the same room, a guard talk passionately, in a Russian accent, about a Durer oil painting of a man with fur draped around his shoulders. "Look at that sable," he said. "It's, like, three dimensional." In most museums, guards stand as stiff as toy soldiers and look as if they are enjoying themselves about as much. It was nice to think of this room watched over by someone in love with art. 
It turned out he was not unique at the Gardner. In the Gothic room I encountered another guard, Frank DiMaria, holding forth beneath Sargent's evocative portrait of Mrs. Gardner. He was gushing about the Rembrandt self-portrait in the room below and gossiping about why the Boston ladies hadn't liked Mrs. Gardner (she bought her dresses in Paris, and was always ahead of local fashion). It sounded as if he had just come from tea with the lady herself. 
I left the Gardner reluctantly and walked back toward my starting point, stopping at the Institute for Contemporary Art on Boylston Street, which featured an installation and landscape photographs by Olafur Eliasson (now closed). It was, quite literally, culture shock to go from the classical forms and images at the Gardner to an installation of a light turning on and off over a floor filled with water. Art endures in part because it always evolves, I thought. 
I then walked a block to Newbury Street, a pedestrian-clotted thoroughfare lined with high-end stores, restaurants, hair salons -- and art galleries, lots of them. I was struck by Laurel Hughes's thickly layered, semi abstract paintings of chickens -- who knew chickens could be rendered so beautifully, and so individually -- at the Nielsen Gallery; Esther Solondz's multimedia work -- one medium being salt, layered on photographs and encrusted on rows of little girls' shoes -- in a ghostly display at Gallery NAGA, which is inside a church; and by paintings exhibited by Paul Shakespear at the Howard Yezerski Gallery. I briefly refueled at Pho Pasteur, a popular Vietnamese restaurant. 
Given the crowds, it was a relief to leave Newbury Street. I walked south, to the South End, where I visited a few galleries in a building in an industrial area at the corner of Harrison and Thayer Streets, including the Kingston Gallery, then walked a ways to the Back Bay-South End MBTA station. An artist friend had told me about a small gallery in the Green Street T station, six stops away in Jamaica Plain. It featured two painters and a sculptor the day I was there, and would be a great starting point for exploring the neighborhood, which features an eclectic mix of artists and immigrants. 
But my legs, my eyes, even my right brain were all crying out for a break. So I went back to my hotel, took a brief nap, then met friends for dinner back in the South End, at Truc, a French bistro with grass-green walls and pressed-tin ceilings. After a good bistro-style dinner, we went across the street to the Mills Gallery at the Boston Center for the Arts, which had an interesting exhibit on handpainted Cambodian shop signs, along with photographs of the signs (including a photograph of a sign-painter's sign). Brightly colored, their Khmer lettering is itself a work of art. And the signs reflect everything from a changing economy to the influence of Hollywood imagery.
A woman I'd met at a gallery during the day had invited me to the opening party for Oni Gallery, a new space renovated by a group of young artists in Chinatown. It was crowded and smoky, and the walls were covered with largely forgettable art. Still, I applauded the effort, then went home to my sheets. 
Sunday morning my friends and I drove to the DeCordova Museum and Sculpture Park, a contemporary art museum, in Lincoln, 16 miles northwest of Boston. 
With snow, mud and ice carpeting the ground, it was a less than ideal day for walking through the outdoor sculpture park, but I saw a few arresting pieces, including Jim Dine's "Two Big Black Hearts," 12 feet high and cast from bronze; Richard Rosen blum's "Venusvine," also bronze, but looking like a wood sea creature rising and twisting on a hill, and John Wilson's bronze head entitled "Eternal Presence." Inside, I loved Adam Cvijanovic's painting and collage "Invisible Sun," and the enormous inflatable nylon sculptures by Pat Oleszko. 
We then went to Boston College's McMullen Museum of Art, which had assembled a rare collection of etchings, lithographs and paintings by Edvard Munch, beginning with tormented self-portraits and ending with pastoral landscapes. Much of the middle section explores Munch's conflicted relations with women. He titled a portrait of a lover "Sin," which pretty much says it all, but also painted idealized images of merging couples. The exhibit runs through May 21.
TIME was running out, and I hadn't been to any of the university museums in Cambridge. I never did make it to the List Visual Arts Center at M.I.T., but I quickly visited the three Harvard art museums, starting with the Fogg Museum, seeing the Van Goghs, Renoirs (compare his self-portrait, at 35, with Aristide Maillol's bust of him 30 years later), Picassos, Monets and a striking painting from Jacob Lawrence's "Struggle" series. 
I forsook the light of Impressionism for the dark of German Expressionism, entering the Busch-Reisinger (access is through the Fogg). The museum is small, but has an excellent collection, including Max Beckmann's brooding tuxedoed self-portrait, Ernst Barlach's visceral sculptures and Kathe Kollwitz's bronze sculpture "Rest in the Peace of His Hands." 
I then rushed to the Arthur M. Sackler Museum down the street. I wandered through lovely collections of Japanese, Chinese and Korean landscape scrolls. Upstairs I passed an intricate Burmese stele with scenes from the life of Buddha, and relief fragments from Persepolis. A weekend looking at art, I thought, is like living in the tension between change and continuity. It is a form of expression that is of its place and age, while simultaneously transcending time, language and culture. (Seeing a great deal of it also requires a certain physical fortitude.) 
As the Sackler began closing, I walked toward the exit, then glanced back. Hanging on the wall was a marble grave stele from Greece, fourth century B.C., of a young girl. A dog lunged playfully toward her; a doll was clutched in her hand. It was such a familiar image, both in the live joy and the frozen grief, that it could have been carved yesterday. It spoke, in a way words never could, across more than a millennium. 

Masters of the past, talents of today

HEADLINE: WHAT'S DOING IN; 
Portland, Ore.

BYLINE:  By MICHAEL UPCHURCH; MICHAEL UPCHURCH is a Seattle novelist who often visits Portland.

 BODY:
In the friendly rivalry between Portland and Seattle, Portland has traditionally played second fiddle in terms of urban vigor and allure. 
 But Seattle has been through a bumpy couple of years, with riots (at the World Trade Organization meeting in December 1999, and at Mardi Gras this year), dot-com layoffs and a 6.8 earthquake giving it the jitters. Ever-increasing traffic congestion and a light-rail system that can't get off the ground don't help. 
 Portland, meanwhile, is bustling confidently. Its light-rail system -- only one route so far, but up and working just fine -- was extended 15 miles westward a few years ago, and a link to the airport is to open in September. A new street car service connecting downtown neighborhoods is due to start in July. 
Other new developments are these: Last August, the Portland Art Museum grew by 60,000 square feet, allowing it to keep most of its permanent collection on display while playing host to touring exhibitions. Powell's City of Books -- a veritable mecca for book lovers -- added two floors to its already vast premises in October, including a splendid rare-book room and an aptly named Basil Hallward Art Gallery (after the artist who painted Dorian Gray's portrait), where visiting authors present readings. The most enticing new arrival may be the Portland Classical Chinese Garden, which offers a taste of botanical-design paradise where a parking lot used to be. 
Some of the rosiness of the City of Roses, as it's called, can be ascribed to its wearing rose-tinted glasses. After all, Portland felt the earthquake, too, and knows it could get more of the same. And it has not been immune to the economic downturn. Finally, like Washington and California, Oregon is facing serious water and power shortages this summer. Still, little of this seems to have affected the town's buoyant mood -- not with all this new stuff to enjoy and show off. 

Events
The Rose Festival, which dominates the city throughout June, starts on May 31 with the coronation of the Rose Festival Queen. She's selected from a Festival Court representing 14 local high schools. The festival continues with fireworks, parades, boat races, performances and art exhibits. Among the highlights this year: Michael Curry, a native Oregonian who designed the puppets and masks for Broadway's "Lion King," will present the world premiere of a multimedia theatrical work, "Spirits," June 7 to 9, at Keller Auditorium, 222 Southwest Clay. Tickets are $15.50 to $39; (503) 224-8499; www.whitebird.org. On June 9 and 10, Ray Charles will appear with the Oregon Symphony at Arlene Schnitzer Concert Hall, 1037 Southwest Broadway; $20 to $75, (800) 228-7343; www.orsymphony.org. 
A Starlight Parade featuring illuminated floats takes place June 2, followed by the climactic Grand Floral Parade on June 9. Seats can be reserved for prime viewing of the Grand Floral Parade ($17 to $20); call (503) 224-4400. Visitors can examine the floats at leisure at the Rose Quarter Commons (between Memorial Coliseum and the Rose Garden Arena) after the parade June 9 and all day June 10 ($3 donation). Or if you prefer roses left on the bush, the Portland Rose Society's 113th Annual Spring Rose Show, billed as the country's largest and longest running, is June 7 and 8 at Lloyd Center across the river ($3). 
Festivities wrap up with a twilight air show June 30 and afternoon air show July 1 at Hillsboro Airport, at Northeast Cornell Road and 25th Street, 15 miles west of downtown. Tickets are $12; reserved seats $17; call (503) 648-1102. Information on all Rose Festival events can be found at www.rosefestival.org, or by calling (503) 227-2681. 
The Rose Festival isn't the only game in town. Oregon Ballet Theater, under the artistic direction of James Canfield, will present its American Choreographers Showcase June 1 to 23, with two alternating programs -- one by women, the other by men. Performances, at Newmark Theater, 1111 Southwest Broadway, are $5 to $80; (888) 922-5538 or www.obt.org. 
The Artists Repertory Theater does good work at bargain prices and in intimate surroundings. Its next production is Warren Leight's jazz era drama "Side Man," May 20 to July 1, at 1516 Southwest Alder Street. Tickets: $15 to $26; (503) 241-1278; www.artistsrep.org. 

Sightseeing
The Portland Art Museum, 1219 Southwest Park Avenue, (503) 226-2811, www.portlandartmuseum.org, is a must this year, and not just because of its recent and welcome expansion. In October, it acquired the complete art collection of the New York critic Clement Greenberg, who died in 1994. His Abstract Expressionist trove goes on display July 14 to Sept. 16, and it will be the only opportunity to see the collection in its entirety before part of it goes on tour. Another museum draw is a specially commissioned "Artifact Panel" by the Northwest glass artist William Morris in the new Hoffman Wing. Visible from three levels, it consists of 399 hand-blown glass objects that masquerade as clay, bone, wood and fossil. Admission: $7.50. 
Billed as the only museum in the country dedicated solely to advertising, the American Advertising Museum, 211 Northwest Fifth Avenue, (503) 226-0000, www.admuseum.org, has also entered a new phase after moving in February to its current premises. A permanent exhibit traces the history of American advertising from 1704 to the present. Memorabilia on display range from Burma Shave signs to ceramic renderings of the Pillsbury Dough Boy. Admission: $5; open Wednesday to Sunday noon to 5 p.m. 
The nearby Portland Classical Chinese Garden, at Northwest Third Avenue and Northwest Everett Street, (503) 228-8131, www .portlandchinesegarden.org, opened last September and occupies an entire city block. It is an urban garden in the 15th-century Ming style, designed and built by architects and artisans from Portland's Chinese sister city, Suzhou. Volunteers give free tours well worth taking. Admission: $6; open 9 a.m. to 6 p.m. daily through Oct. 31; Nov. 1 through March 31, 10 a.m. to 5 p.m. daily. 
Garden lovers will also want to visit the Japanese Garden, 611 Southwest Kingston Avenue, (503) 223-4070, which covers five hilly acres offering stunning views of the city, Mount Hood and Mount St. Helens. Admission, $6; 10 a.m. to 7 p.m. daily through Sept. 30 (Monday noon to 7 p.m.); Oct. 1 through March 31, 10 a.m. to 4 p.m. (Monday noon to 4 p.m.). The nearby International Rose Test Gardens, 400 Southwest Kingston Avenue, (503) 823-3636, are part of Washington Park and one of the test sites for the American Rose Society, with more than 500 varieties. Hours: 7 a.m. to 9 p.m. daily; free.
The Willamette River runs through the city, and you can meander with it through Waterfront Park, a project from the 1970's that opened Portland's downtown core to the waterway. The Portland Spirit, (800) 224-3901, www.portlandspirit .com, departs from the park near the end of Salmon Street and goes upriver, passing the city's fanciest riverside residences along the way. A variety of cruise packages on the 150-foot yacht are offered, ranging from a simple sightseeing tour ($15) to a champagne brunch buffet cruise ($36) to dinner cruises ($52).

Where to Stay
The latest arrival on the downtown scene, the 154-room Paramount Hotel, 808 Southwest Taylor Street, (503) 223-9900, fax (503) 223-7900, www.paramounthotel.net, which opened in January 2000, boasts a central location, friendly service and a light and airy feel to its rooms, some of which have terraces. While it can't quite match the character of Portland's older hotels, it's very comfortable and has a fresh-faced appeal. Doubles are $195, with special rates starting at $129.
Two older hotels offer a cozier charm. The 107-room Hotel Vintage Plaza, 422 Southwest Broadway, (800) 243-0555, fax (503) 228-3598, www.vintageplaza.com, was built in 1894 and refurbished in the late 1990's. The eclectic selection of accommodations includes some unhotel-like solarium-windowed Starlight rooms on the top floor. Doubles are $109 to $229. 
It's hard to believe, but 10 years ago the 100-room Governor Hotel, 611 Southwest 10th Avenue, (800) 554-3456, fax (503) 241-2122, www .govhotel.com, was a dilapidated shell that served as a set for Gus Van Sant's film "My Own Private Idaho." Now it's a swank affair with a lobby, featuring a Lewis and Clark mural, that's worth a stop. Rooms, some with terraces, are bright and comfortable. The gym has a pool and indoor track; $8 usage fee. Doubles: $185 to $195. 
Budget: The 130-room Mallory Hotel, 729 Southwest 15th Avenue, (800) 228-8657, fax (503) 223-0522, www .malloryhotel.com, set back several blocks from downtown across Interstate 405, is an old favorite among bargain-minded visitors. Its bar, a former sauna, is all curvy brick and shadowed recesses. Doubles: $95 to $155.
Another option is the Mark Spencer Hotel, 409 Southwest 11th Avenue, (800) 548-3934, fax (503) 223-7848, www.markspencer.com, close to the nightclub district. If you plan on making noise on the weekend instead of listening to it, it's a great deal. Each of the 101 rooms in this former apartment building comes with a full kitchen. Doubles: from $89. 
Luxury: The Benson, 309 Southwest Broadway, (503) 228-2000, fax (503) 471-3920, www.bensonhotel .com, built in 1912, is the ultimate in opulence of the timber baron era. The lobby, paneled in rare Circassian walnut, is plush and shadowy, illuminated by chandeliers. The 287 rooms are modest in size but tastefully and comfortably furnished. Doubles are $175 to $255, but packages can start as low as $155. 

Where to Eat
Southpark Seafood Grill and Wine Bar, 901 Southwest Salmon Street, (503) 326-1300, is a visual knockout: high-ceilinged with dark-hued walls of red and brown and some energetic cafe society murals behind the bar. The ahi tuna au poivre and the salads and bread (served with white bean spread) are a delight. The restaurant offers frequent tastings (book ahead). Dinner for two with wine: $100. 
Overlooking the RiverPlace Marina, McCormick and Schmick's Harborside Restaurant and Pilsner Room, 0309 Southwest Montgomery Street, (503) 220-1865, is ideal for lunch. Tables rise in tiers, guaranteeing a view of river activity. Try the Southwest salmon cakes, light and flavorful with a sweet red-pepper crunch. The seared rare ahi with pickled ginger and wasabi is also a must. Dinner for two with wine: $90. 
One of the latest arrivals on the restaurant scene, in the heart of the city's frenziedly gentrifying Pearl District, is Bluehour, 250 Northwest 13th Avenue; (503) 226-3394. Here, former warehouse digs have been turned into an imaginative stage set, with floor-to-ceiling curtains dividing up a big boxy space into dining and bar areas. The green salad, tomato and lentil soup, potato gnocchi and grilled chicken sandwich are excellent. Dinner for two with wine is about $100. 
Abou Karim, 221 Southwest Pine Street, (503) 223-5058, serves Lebanese cuisine that's fresh, aromatic and delectable. Along with the usual kebabs, the house specialty is the mezza: a sampler of hummus, tabbouleh, baba ghanouj, falafel and other dishes. Dinner for two with wine: $50. 
Typhoon!, with branches at 2310 Northwest Everett Street, (503) 243-7557, and 400 Southwest Broadway, (503) 224-8285, offers Thai food, stylishly served, with prices under $10 for many dinner entrees.

 CORRECTION:
A chart on April 29 with the What's Doing column, about Portland, Ore., referred incorrectly to the cost of a dinner for one. It did include a tip, but not a meal tax; Oregon does not charge one.

HEADLINE: TRAVEL ADVISORY: CORRESPONDENT'S REPORT; 
Dude Ranches Face Foot-and-Mouth Fears

BYLINE:  By EDWIN McDOWELL 

 BODY:
TWELVE guests from Britain have already canceled their reservations for this spring and summer at the Hideout at Flitner Ranch in Shell, Wyo., for fear that if they were exposed to foot-and-mouth disease, they might bring it to the United States. And right now, it's anybody's guess whether other foreign tourists will show up at the Hideout as scheduled.
 Although there are no signs of the disease in the United States -- a disease that is devastating to cattle and other livestock but rarely affects humans -- many Europeans who have been canceling say they do not want to risk the possibility of spreading it to America. Moreover, ranchers say that many callers have confused foot-and-mouth disease with mad cow disease, which can be fatal to humans. 
 In many areas of Britain, the highly contagious disease has ravaged herds of cattle and flocks of sheep. Some dude ranchers with sizable herds of cattle have banned European guests this year, for fear that their livestock will become infected with the virus, which clings to shoes and clothing. For example, Fortune Ranch Adventures in Belvidere, S.D., which has about 1,000 cattle, has canceled bookings of about 20 British guests who were to arrive throughout this summer and into the fall.
"The cancellations will cost us about $20,000, or about half of what it costs us to live all year," said Bob Fortune, the owner. "The odds that visitors will bring the disease from Europe are probably less than 1 percent. But when you have a $1 million investment, you can't take the risk." 
The United States Department of Agriculture warned last month that should foot-and-mouth break out here, "the economic impact could reach billions of dollars in the first year." 
With 1,200 head of cattle, the Hideout, a 95-year-old ranch at the foot of the Big Horn Mountains in north-central Wyoming, would also be vulnerable. But it hasn't had to cancel bookings since most Europeans canceled on their own. "It's going to be a tough year," said Julie Cote, director of marketing and hospitality at the 250,000-acre ranch.
With weekly rates ranging from $1,000 to $2,000 a person, and most such ranches open to tourists an average of only 21 weeks a year, according to the Dude Ranchers Association of North America, that represents considerable lost revenue. What's more, the possibility that foot-and-mouth disease could turn up in the United States is nightmarish for many dude ranchers, especially after last year's disastrous summer. Wildfires ruined much of the tourist season throughout the West, huge areas of park land and hiking trails were closed, gasoline prices across the country began their steady climb, and the nation's economy began to soften.
Now this. Dude ranches draw only a tiny share of the tourist market -- about 86,000 visitors a year, according to Bobbi Futterer, co-executive director of the Dude Ranchers Association, which has about 115 members in 12 Western states and 2 Canadian provinces. Of that total, Mrs. Futterer said, only about 10 percent are foreigners. And while apparently no reliable studies exist of the economic impact of dude ranch vacations, the impact on individual ranches and local communities is not inconsiderable.
So far, the majority of dude ranches appear to be accepting bookings from Europeans. Sylvan Dale Guest Ranch in Loveland, Colo., which is also a working cattle ranch, is taking them, as is Lozier's Box "R" Ranch in Cora, Wyo. "We're happy to have them," said David Jessup, an owner of Sylvan Dale, "but we're concerned about bringing the disease into our herd." 
Both ranches expect visitors to follow the guidelines spelled out in advisories issued by the Agriculture Department. 
These suggest that visitors avoid contact with livestock or wildlife for five days before traveling to the United States and five days after arriving. They also suggest that before foreign guests leave home, they clean all clothing and outerwear, remove soil from shoes then wipe them with a cloth dampened with a solution of five teaspoons of household bleach in a gallon of water. 
Some guest ranches that haven't experienced wholesale cancellations take little comfort in that, since they have had few European bookings to begin with. "As far as I know, nobody in this area has had any trouble with cancellations," said an employee at the Ironhorse Ranch in Tombstone, Ariz., who would not be identified. 
She also said that things have been "a little slow so far this year," but added that October is usually the biggest month for German visitors. But whether that will be true this year will likely depend on what happens with what the Agriculture Department said is one of the animal diseases that livestock owners dread most.
Surprisingly perhaps, some tour operators abroad say that they still expect the usual complement of foreign visitors at dude ranches this summer, at least if the disease does not show up in the United States. Ron Harris, the owner of Ranch America -- a big tour operator in Hertfordshire, England, which sends several million tourists to the United States each year, including about 2,500 to dude ranches -- said Europeans still want to visit. "They still have this fantasy to see and do things that John Wayne saw and did," he said. 
But the last outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease in the United States occurred in 1929, so if any of John Wayne's characters had to deal with it, most Americans have not.
