HEADLINE: Those Little Town Blues, in Old New York?; 
Giuliani's Capital of the World, Insisting It's Greater Than Fort Worth

BYLINE:  By PETER APPLEBOME 

 BODY:
Perhaps the worst sports event in history played out in Dallas's unrelenting triple-digit heat in July 1984 during the running of the Dallas Grand Prix, a Formula One auto race meant to bring the cosmopolitan panache of Monte Carlo to the steamy streets around the Cotton Bowl.
 Sweltering race fans collapsed in the stands. So many cars overheated and broke down that only 8 of 25 finished the race. One driver was treated for heat exhaustion when he collapsed while trying to push his car across the finish line. It was, in short, an utter triumph. "If there is any doubt in anyone's mind that we are truly an international city, we certainly have dispelled that," Mayor Starke Taylor exulted. 
 Reasonable minds may differ on whether New York has joined Dallas in the ranks of truly international cities. But when Mayor Starke, er, Rudolph W. Giuliani last week unveiled plans for what is informally being called the Tour de Manhattan, a 100-kilometer bicycle race to be held in Lower Manhattan a week after the Tour de France, it raised an intriguing question: How come New York, once lordly and imperious, now finds itself regularly competing on the promotional turf of third-tier cities where a big night out means dinner at the Olive Garden and taking in "Freddy Got Fingered" at the mall multiplex? 
Or to put it bluntly: New York, hick town or truly international city?
You make the call.
"We're imitating the tawdry events of second- and third-class cities; I don't know why," said Marshall Blonsky, a professor of communications at New York University and a senior fellow at the New School University. "They're putting on events to show they're world class, when they're not, and we're imitating second-rate cities pretending to be first rate. It's really quite curious."
Of course, the race -- blending the current fitness vogue; a worthy beneficiary, Memorial Sloan-Kettering Cancer Center; and a TV-ready locale -- may be a big hit.
And if any American city can claim to be truly international, it is New York, where cabdrivers can get you lost in 132 different languages and dialects.
But to look at the municipal agenda of recent weeks, it can be hard to see much that would not be at home in Indianapolis or Charlotte.
There is the mayor's raging stadium envy; stadiums have long been the top agenda item for towns and cities on the make from Arlington, Tex., to Pittsburgh. In the interim, New Yorkers can get excited about the new minor league teams and stadiums scheduled for Brooklyn and Staten Island.
There's angst about the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center and the city's ability to compete with Chicago and Atlanta for conventions of cosmetologists, anthropologists and plumbing supply manufacturers.
The mayor is also doing his best to gin up his decency panel to screen out offensive works of art, a concept reminiscent of Dallas's Motion Picture Classification Board, which for 27 years scoured newly released films for offensive content until the city got rid of it almost a decade ago. Eager-to-please urban attractions, like the Chelsea Piers, hustle to offer suburban pleasures like driving ranges. And there is Times Square, once the quintessence of urban sin, now a suburb of Orlando.
There are two seemingly contradictory trends in play.
The first is the triumph of nice. We have a nice president and first lady. Demographic trends continue to favor nice places like Salt Lake City, Phoenix or Atlanta over gritty ones like the older industrial cities of the Northeast. It is probably no accident that New York shrank during the nasty old "Bonfire of the Vanities" days and began to grow again when people from Birmingham and Dubuque visited and went home saying that New Yorkers weren't so bad. People want an urban experience, but a nice one -- "Friends" and "You've Got Mail," not "Taxi Driver" and "Blade Runner."
Second is the mania for being a Truly International City. When Atlanta was awarded the 1996 Olympics, the delirious headline in the newspapers crowed: "World Class." In Charlotte, where The Charlotte Observer is forever running articles with headlines like "Charlotte Hits Big Time: We Need 2 Phone Books," people speculate, at least half seriously, that the local newspaper has a policy that the words "world class" or "truly international city" must run at least once a day.
Ever since Atlanta snared the Olympics, half the cities of America -- New York included -- have been dreaming of doing the same thing or trying to produce extravaganzas with just the right aroma of world-classiness.
New York officials hit the same notes.
"New York City is the greatest city in the world, and the sports capital of the world," said Kenneth J. Podziba, the city's sports commissioner. "We feel we're the best, but we can always get better, which is what we're trying to do."
Many people have a simple explanation for New York's recent behavior. It begins with a "G." "New York had a long, proud tradition of boasting only of its unlivability, but that was before Giuliani," said Ann Douglas, a writer who teaches American studies at Columbia. "New York is a world city in a way no other American city can be. It doesn't need to boast. But I think the city's more rooted incarnation is an anti-Giulianian one. Cities go through climatic periods, and we're going through one period now, but it will change again."
Others see more substantial trends.
Phillip Lopate, the author who teaches at Hofstra University, said that with the decline of manufacturing and the port, New York now competes regularly for business with cities it once disdained. And in that competition, cities are selling images as much as realities.
"In the post-industrial period, cities have to position themselves as cultural flytraps and to market themselves based on images; the image of city life becomes a product of the city," he said. "If you use the canyons of Wall Street as a resonant stage set for a race, it's what we're doing all the time here: holding onto the old built environment and putting it to a new use. It's part of the image we're selling."
And Kenneth T. Jackson, editor of The Encyclopedia of New York City and a history professor at Columbia, said that New York would be foolish not to preen and market itself as aggressively as its less-lofty competitors. "Part of the charm of New York may be that we don't give a damn about a lot of things that other cities care about, but the fact is cities are in a very competitive environment, and in some ways we don't compete as well," he said. "Places like Dallas and Atlanta and Houston have done a really good job in the last half century of selling themselves, and New York has to sell, too."
And, he added, for most of its history New York has hardly been a lofty sanctuary for artistes and litterateurs, but the world's most tenacious capital of commerce and avarice, a place where Charlotte's cut-to-the-chase, mid-70's civic slogan, "Charlotte -- A Good Place to Make Money," would not have been inappropriate.
There are, of course, questions about how much promotional events really produce. Atlanta got a new stadium, road improvements and some student housing out of the Olympics. It also saw its downtown compared to a flea market and an unsolved bombing that marred the games.
Indeed, civic boosters have produced more than their share of flops. 
A chapter of boosterism run amuck could be devoted to the Spring Palace, an exhibit hall and tourist attraction in Fort Worth, Tex., built in 1889 at a cost of $100,000. Every inch of the structure was covered with Texas flora like wheat, corn, cactus, rye, moss and Johnson grass. A local historian modestly described it as "easily the most beautiful structure ever erected on earth," until it burned to the ground a year later. Apparently it had not occurred to anyone that a building made out of oats, popcorn and Johnson grass might be a fire hazard.
Still, Professor Jackson said, from Fort Worth in the 19th century to New York in the 21st, cities rest on their laurels at their peril. Which is why what promises to be (A) the ultimate urban extravaganza or (B) the ultimate urban doomsday machine -- New York's bid for the 2012 Olympics -- continues to plow ahead.
"The entrepreneurial spirit of New York got the Erie Canal built, the stock exchange; nothing came out of whole cloth," Professor Jackson said. "A bike race may be lame compared to the Erie Canal, but you've got to think of something."

 Late Edition - Final

SECTION: Section 6; Column 1; Magazine Desk; Pg. 58

LENGTH: 3142 words

HEADLINE: Strom In the Balance 

BYLINE:  By James Bennet; James Bennet is a staff writer for the magazine.

 BODY:
In his front-row seat in the Senate chamber, Strom Thurmond sits with mouth slackly agape. His thin, rust-colored hair is stretched back from the top of his head, so straight that some strands do not follow the curve of his skull but float out behind it. His hands have grown enormous, spidery, and the fingers don't bend as easily as they once did at knuckles that have come to look like walnuts. Those hands were cut up in the crash landing of an Army glider flown over the bloody Normandy beaches; Thurmond was already 44 years old then. Now he occasionally raises them to touch his eyebrows, his cheeks and his lips with his index fingers, straight as chopsticks, or to pat that wayward hair. As his name approaches in the roll call -- around about the R's -- the aide seated beside him leans over and whispers urgently in his ear. Then, as the senator's name is called, the aide taps him on the elbow, and Thurmond rises to his feet to cry out, "No!" 
 It has come to this. Through a lifetime that witnessed the extension of the vote to women, the advent of television and jet travel, Prohibition and its repeal, the liberation of Buchenwald, the birth of the Soviet Union and its death, the crash of '29 and the Internet bubble, Strom Thurmond has been many things: progressive educator, New Dealer, soldier, race-baiter, presidential aspirant, Goldwater Republican and husband of beauty queens. Now he is the oldest senator ever. His prized dignity under siege, he is the subject of a death watch by his colleagues on Capitol Hill and his fellow politicians in South Carolina, his accumulated influence exercised by his estranged wife and loyal staff in a prolonged anticlimax that is by turns tenderly gothic and cruelly antic, part "As I Lay Dying" and part "Weekend at Bernie's." 
 Yet what is most striking about this 98-year-old man, who as president pro tempore of the Senate is third in line for the presidency, is not how ancient he looks but how well he suits his environment, like the brass spittoons lingering beneath some varnished desks. He is at the faded edge of the gray continuum that is the United States Senate. His extreme case has masked the advancing ages of his colleagues, seven of whom are more than 70 years old. The junior senator from South Carolina, Fritz Hollings, was born in 1922. Behind Thurmond and to his left sits Jesse Helms of neighboring North Carolina, pale and shiny as a shelled egg, his four-legged cane beside him. He was born 14 presidents ago, in 1921. Across the floor is Robert Byrd, natty as ever in brown suit and brown and beige dappled vest, thick white hair swept up and back, sharp eyes roving. Born 1917. And here, moving surprisingly stiffly and slowly, comes leonine John Warner of Virginia, the man who replaced Thurmond as chairman of the Senate Armed Services Committee a couple of years ago when Thurmond acknowledged that he had aged beyond the task. Warner was born in 1927.
The younger senators move briskly among their older colleagues like cheerful, efficient nurses. When he spies one of them, Thurmond leans over to his aide, apparently to confirm that the blonde is, in fact, Hillary Rodham Clinton. He reaches into his desk drawer and, slowly working those chopsticks, extracts something from a plastic bag. When she comes within range, he presses against the arms of his chair and raises himself slowly to his feet. Helped by his aide, he totters over to Clinton, seizes her right hand with his left and gives her the bag's contents: a couple of pieces of candy.
It may be hard sometimes to escape the impression that the Senate chamber resembles the fossil-strewn reading room of some Ivy League club, occupied by alumni from before the Depression, men with rich lives behind them and no place else to go. But it is equally hard to escape the judgment of which institution does better at capitalizing on their experience. Offering excellent health care, the stimulating presence of young people, a range of activities from simple to complex and relevancy, the Senate is the most effective nursing home in the world, which no doubt helps explain why Thurmond has lived so long. 
And while the Senate is good for the aged, on balance, they are good for it. Of all the rapacious influences to which senators are vulnerable, age is properly far down the list of the Republic's worries.
With the Senate split in half and Vice President Dick Cheney available to break any tie, Republicans are desperate to keep Thurmond voting. Democrats, meanwhile, tried to slow down the budget process in hopes that they might gain another vote if Jim Hodges, the Democratic governor of South Carolina, got the chance to appoint a replacement. Yet it is one of the strange consequences of his long career that Thurmond seems as popular these days among his Democratic colleagues as among the Republicans. Republican and Democrat, senators stop in the chamber to greet him. A few seem eager to move on, but others stoop as Thurmond clutches at their forearms, for they know that though he is too vain to use a hearing aid most of the time, he can still converse. As Helms shuffles past to vote, he pats Thurmond's shoulder, then pumps his right fist skyward in an age-defying gesture of encouragement and solidarity.
Thurmond, his associates say, has his good days and bad days. No one pretends that he is fully up to the job. Earlier this session, he gave up a task he loved: calling the Senate to order. He still votes regularly, but those eloquent remarks attributed to him in the Senate record are not uttered on the floor but submitted by his staff. Because of what his office calls fatigue, he has been back and forth in the past year to Walter Reed Hospital. His attendance at hearings has become spotty, like his memory, his friends say, of events after 1984. For the first time, he is receiving assistance at his condominium in nearby Alexandria, Va. Clinging to two aides, he takes six-inch steps through the Capitol. As he minces, the rumors race -- from senators to Congressional aides to reporters: Strom is back in the hospital! Strom has liver cancer! Thurmond partisans roll their eyes and note that the graveyards are full of people waiting for him to die.
Is he physically weaker?" asks a senator who sees Thurmond frequently. "Yeah, a lot weaker." But, saying that he had not detected any new mental weakness, this senator adds: "I'm not prepared to say he's not cognizant of how he's voting. He's just getting more tired. It's harder to walk; it's harder to stay steady."
As a young man, just to prove he could do it, Thurmond ran 20 miles in canvas sneakers, continuing even after his toenails came off. A fitness buff who forswore coffee and tobacco and who seldom drank, he has always been proud of his toughness and physical strength. He still refuses to use a wheelchair in the Senate, though he sometimes uses one privately. His physical decline, therefore, is particularly poignant. "There's a term out here now that's never been heard before about Strom, that he's 'weak,"' says Jack Bass, co-author with Marilyn Thompson of the biography "Ol' Strom."
Hard as it has been to witness that deterioration, however, what has particularly saddened Thurmond's friends and admirers has been a series of embarrassing revelations and denials involving his second wife, Nancy. The senator was 66 when he married Nancy Moore, a 22-year-old former Miss South Carolina who had been an intern in his office. Dropping out of law school shortly before her wedding, Nancy Thurmond became an effective campaigner and influential adviser for her husband, and there was talk in political circles that she might run for his seat when he eventually retired. But he didn't. In 1991, the couple separated, and in 1993, their eldest daughter was killed by a drunken driver. Three years later, Nancy Thurmond was arrested for driving drunk, and she later admitted to struggling with alcohol and diet pills.
Beginning early last year, rumors circulated among South Carolina politicians that Nancy Thurmond, now 54, was interested in replacing her husband. In November, Senator Thurmond remarked to a reporter that he might retire early so that his estranged wife could serve out his term. "I wouldn't object to serving two or three months less if she'd like to do it, if that would make her happy," he said. "She's been with me all these years, worked hard."
Of course, if Thurmond stepped down, it would not be up to him but to Governor Hodges to pick his replacement. Once the report of the senator's comment appeared, Hodges's office said that a month earlier Nancy Thurmond had asked the governor to appoint her to her husband's seat but that he had demurred. Nancy Thurmond denied making that request, and the senator issued a statement saying he would serve out his term, which ends in 2002.
Fast-forward to February. The State newspaper in Columbia reported that at her meeting with the governor, Nancy Thurmond played a videotape of the senator supporting her as his successor. That report came on top of news that the senator had nominated his 28-year-old son, Strom Jr., or Little Strom as he is known in South Carolina, to be the state's next United States attorney. And it came on top of a letter to the White House on Thurmond's stationery reportedly requesting a job interview on behalf of Dr. Robert Oldham, following up on a request that had been made to the Bush team by telephone. Oldham is chairman of the South Carolina Biotechnology Association, of which Nancy Thurmond is executive director, and he is also delicately identified as her "social companion" in the local press. Suddenly, Strom Thurmond was being criticized back home. It seemed for the first time that he was out to feather his nest -- or that he was being used.
Hodges refuses to comment on the videotape, and Nancy Thurmond refuses to grant interview requests. "That's all been covered in previous statements" was all Oldham would tell me. That was apparently a reference to a lengthy statement issued last month by Nancy Thurmond, in which she denied that she had any interest in replacing her husband. She said that the videotape was a thank-you to South Carolina, to be shown in the event of a disabling illness or death, and that she had helped him prepare it without the knowledge of his staff. She also said that she had called and written the White House not to get Oldham a job but to schedule a meeting for him to give advice on drug development.
Thurmond, some of his associates say, remains very protective of Nancy and even hopeful that she will come back to him. One friend of the family says that he hopes the senator is not following the news about her. "It would be terribly offensive to Strom," he says. "He's always been supermacho about himself."
But another, closer, friend, Armstrong Williams, the syndicated columnist, says that the senator raised the subject with him in mid-March: "He asked me in the office, 'What are we going to do about Nancy?' and he chuckled." Thurmond, he says, was concerned about her dignity, not at all worried about his own. 
Hodges, who has had a rocky relationship with the national Democratic Party, refuses to say whom he would appoint. He will not even indicate what party he would turn to, only that he would seek a "caretaker," someone unlikely to run for a full term. "I think it's tacky to talk about a vacancy that doesn't exist," he says. "I believe he'll continue to serve in that seat until the term is up." The chairman of the state Democratic Party, Dick Harpootlian, says that he has no doubt that Hodges will pick a Democrat in the end. Fearing that, Republicans in the State Assembly passed a bill to compel him to pick a Republican. But the measure has little support in the State Senate.
Even Democrats in Columbia seem sick of the spotlight on the senior senator. "Austrian television is in town," Harpootlian snorts as he sorts through a stack of telephone messages. "Reference Sen. Thurmond."' State Senator John Courson, a fellow Republican and self-described disciple of Thurmond's, expresses dismay about the media attention. "It takes away from this incredible aura of public service and courageous public stances he's taken over the years," he says. Courson, who last spoke with Thurmond when the senator was in Columbia at Christmas, insists that if Thurmond ever doubts he can make the right decisions about his voting, he will resign. But when I ask if Thurmond is capable of making such a judgment about himself, Courson replies, "That's a very good question." He pauses for a long moment. "I would say yes," he finally says.
But what difference would it make if the answer was no? All senators are only as good as their aides -- and each has an average of 43 of them, not counting the committee staff. Every senator, at least sometimes, relies on staff members or colleagues to guide votes. Senators count on their aides to clean up or even create their remarks in the official record, to brush the dandruff from their shoulders, to give them the background on the next donor they must call. Thurmond has merely plumbed the reductio ad absurdum of such enabling -- actually, others have plunged deeper, not showing up to vote for years -- and if his constituents don't mind, why should anyone else? In 1996, they re-elected him by a comfortable margin to what he said would be his last term. Some South Carolina politicians say that Thurmond could be re-elected even if he were dead.
Which raises an interesting question: Why is this old man, who has seldom visited home or addressed his constituents in recent years, so popular? His popularity's endurance does not have much to do with legislative leadership. Thurmond has little to show, legislatively, for his 46 years in the Senate. When I ask for an example of a significant law that would not have passed without Thurmond's influence, Thomas Mann, an expert on the Congress at the Brookings Institution, is stumped. "He's just never been a major player in the body," he says.
Jack Bass, Thurmond's biographer, argues that the senator was critical in blocking the appointment of Abe Fortas as chief justice of the United States, which delayed the retirement of Earl Warren and ultimately gave Nixon a crucial extra appointment to the Supreme Court. And, Bass says, Thurmond also blocked George Wallace's insurgency, handing Nixon the presidency in 1968. Fair enough -- but both of those achievements came before the Beatles broke up.
While he may not have dominated the Senate, Thurmond has worked every obscure South Carolina fair for decades. Until recently, every child who graduated from high school received a congratulatory letter from the senator, and it seems sometimes as if every one of them went on to intern in his office. In 1996, Thurmond's Democratic opponent, Elliott Close, polled voters with an unusual question: How many of them had met the senator face to face? A whopping 68 percent said that they had. Thurmond's Senate office is still plugging away, helping state residents with their taxes, health benefits and legal troubles. The office has also continued to push for federal projects in the state, like financing for a proposed plutonium treatment plant at the Savannah River nuclear complex. 
Yet Thurmond's popularity seems to spring less from what he does than from who he is. His sulfurous Dixiecrat campaign for president is all but forgotten. Astonishingly, it was omitted, with his permission, from a list of achievements beneath a statue of Thurmond recently erected outside the Statehouse in Columbia. More enduring has been Thurmond's reputation for incorruptibility and unpretentiousness, together with his image as a rake, which has done him great political good. In "Ol' Strom," Bass and Thompson reported that in his 40's Thurmond seduced a convict on her way to the electric chair. His constituents and fellow senators love to retail stories about his attentions to the ladies, including one, which he denied, that he groped a fellow senator, Patty Murray of Washington. ("Are you married, little lady?" he was reported by Senator Barbara Boxer to have inquired mid-grope.) Thurmond gets to have such stories both ways. They do not hurt him, because he is too old to be seen as a predator. At the same time, they make him seem virile despite his age.
And at bottom, it is longevity that has made Thurmond a "legend," the label inevitably applied to him. He is popular because he is still alive. In 1996, Close tried to turn Thurmond's age against him but found instead that it cut the other way. Kevin Geddings, Close's media consultant, kept hearing the same response from focus groups to suggestions that Thurmond was no longer up to the job: "He's been giving his whole self to the state for 70 years, and if he wants to serve out the rest of his life in office, who are we not to give him that?" Williams says his fellow Carolinians have come to regard Thurmond as a link with fading traditions and even as a biblical figure. "They say, 'The Lord must have saw fit for him to live so long.' It's mystical, mythical." 
Thurmond's most lasting accomplishment may be just that -- lasting. "I wish I had his bloodline," says Senator Joe Biden, the Delaware Democrat (born 1942). "He's incredible. He is absolutely, mind-bogglingly incredible." Thurmond is by most accounts not an introspective man or an enthusiastic storyteller -- he has never wanted to waste time with such chitchat -- and he appears to have left no comprehensive account of the extraordinary change he witnessed, at least not yet. Still, he is wondered at for having witnessed so much. Harpootlian, who came to know and admire Thurmond while prosecuting the driver who killed his daughter, marvels that the senator in his youth knew veterans of the Civil War. "Some of the people he talked to, when they were young, they had seen Andrew Jackson," he says. "I mean, I talked to a guy, who talked to a guy, who had seen Andrew Jackson!"
Of course, the same, or similar, things could be said of many very old people moldering in rest homes, unasked about what they might have seen or whom they might have spoken to. But where longevity usually visits cruelties upon the aged, it has instead bestowed kindnesses on Thurmond. It has made him more popular, not less. It has given more heft to every word he says. It has brought him celebrity, not obscurity. Being so very old has, in sum, made Strom Thurmond powerful. "Strom takes on an importance individually because of his possible departure that he never had as a member of the Senate," Mann says. In his frailty, Thurmond is looming larger as a presence in the Senate than he ever did in his prime. He has lasted long enough that he is famous and popular, in the end, for having lasted so long. And his weakness has made him strong.

HEADLINE: Presidents Who Aim High 

BYLINE:  By Newt Gingrich; Newt Gingrich, who was speaker of the House from 1995 through 1998, is the chief executive of the Gingrich Group, a communications and management consulting firm.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON 

 BODY:
As analysts dissect President Bush's first hundred days, the most important thing to remember is that he and his administration are different from what Washington has become used to. Because Mr. Bush works in a disciplined way to implement a broad strategy, he has been able to set the stage calmly and methodically for a potentially far-reaching performance.
 Not insignificantly, the biggest changes in Washington have been cultural rather than political: the atmosphere is more businesslike, and dialogue across party lines is calmer. President Bush has already been able to change the tone dramatically, even after the 35-day, often toxic fight over the election. He has been friendly, flexible, open and very conservative -- though no reporter seems to be capable of typing those four adjectives in one sentence. Mr. Bush is willing to delegate important initiatives and decisions and give credit to others. 
 The president remains focused on the themes he campaigned on: cutting taxes, reforming education, expanding access to health care while reforming Medicare, creating personal Social Security accounts, and strengthening our military and intelligence capabilities. The only new theme to emerge in the first 100 days is the concept of the Free Trade Area of the Americas. Labor unions and the Democrats in Congress will create the opportunity and the political necessity for President Bush to engage the Hispanic community to help build real commitment to the free trade area. The debate is likely to combine diplomatic, economic and political factors enabling the Bush administration to demonstrate its commitment to free trade while emphasizing the benefits to citizens in all of the Americas.
President Bush has assembled an exceptionally competent, mature and experienced team, but while the strength of his cabinet and advisers is their ability to remain focused on large goals, they face the challenge of creating a shared commitment with the American people. Undoubtedly, the Bush team can manage the daily government and meet international challenges. The question is whether it can reach out to the country and arouse the level of support his bold proposals will require.
The Bush team is still wrestling with the objective reality that this is not a Franklin Roosevelt or Ronald Reagan era of obvious change. F. D. R. had the Great Depression and the Second World War as backdrops. Ronald Reagan inherited economic decay and the most intense Soviet effort to win the cold war. These were great administrations, but they were in times of response to great events.
Mr. Bush has been effectively barnstorming the country for his program, and this tactic is clearly working for him personally, but it may or may not be working for his programs. People see his steady activity and are impressed that he is a serious person with a real plan. Still, they are not yet convinced that they have something real at stake or that his solutions to public policy problems could dramatically change their lives for the better. Consequently, the public hasn't become engaged and hasn't put pressure to bear on Congress to get the Bush program passed.
Dwight Eisenhower was very effective in managing the system, but in the end he transformed neither his party nor the country in domestic policy. It was Barry Goldwater and Mr. Reagan, in the cold war years, who transformed the Republican Party and ultimately the country.
The last Republican president to transform the country and his party in prosperous and peaceful times was Theodore Roosevelt. He did so by knowing the difference between managerial politics and transformational politics. He knew that managing Washington was a minimalist approach. He focused on rallying the country to impose on Washington changes that reactionaries in both parties vehemently opposed.
A transformational presidency has to convince the American people that it is in their best interest to implement its plans. Unless the Bush administration can do this, it will be very hard to get Congress to make transformational changes. The morning the president convinces his party and the country that his goals are decisive in their lives and that they have to join him in insisting that Washington implement them, he will have begun to transform the system.
The environment has been the most obvious public relations failure of the administration so far, and it is also a good issue on which to note the difference between managerial and transformational styles. The Bush administration dislikes the regulatory, adversarial, litigious, government-dominated model of environmentalism that is the hallmark of liberal groups. That wing of the environmental movement is an adversary of business, free markets, private property and full-time rural residents. It favors bureaucrats, trial lawyers, government control of daily life (what kind of car you drive, what kind of gas you buy, where you are allowed to drive, what you can do with your own farm or ranch), and it favors the affluent transient rural residents who earn a living in places like Hollywood but tell Idahoans and Montanans how to live.
President Bush and Vice President Richard Cheney represent a Western distrust of Washington-controlled, bureaucratic policies. The Bush cabinet includes veterans of state government and business who know full well that the Washington-based environmental bureaucracies are politicized, anti-business, anti-local rights, and often extreme in the policies they implement. Yet the political climate is such that the Bush team cannot simply ignore the arguments of the most vocal activists in the environmental movement.
This will force a real choice on the Bush administration. A managerial, Eisenhower-style approach would create the most conservative regulatory policies that the current political system could tolerate. It would then grudgingly give the left those environmental victories it could not block. A transformational, Theodore Roosevelt-style approach would develop a vision of a healthy environment with maximum biodiversity that would attract the support of the vast majority of Americans and would use a high-technology, scientifically based, locally implemented and cooperative approach to problem solving.
The shift from a public presumption of the left's moral superiority has already occurred in education, health and social security. President Bush can make policy proposals in those areas that would have been unthinkable 20 years ago, like personal social security accounts and school choice. Today there is an opportunity for similar transformation on environmental issues.
The key to the first term will be this distinction between a managerial and a transformational approach. President Bush is a good manager, and he has assembled a good team of managers. However, his goals are far beyond the reach of normal management. Achieving his agenda will require the kind of national outpouring of support that is normally aroused by transformational strategies. It will be interesting to see how the two blend together in the second hundred days.

HEADLINE: Liberties; 
I Have a Nickname!!!

BYLINE:  By MAUREEN DOWD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON 

 BODY:
So I found myself on Friday in the Cobra Reading Room on the Web.
 I had discovered on the Hotline, the Racing Form of politics, that President Bush's nickname for me is "The Cobra." 
 I wasn't sure if that was an insult or a sign of respect. So I figured I'd read up on some cobra traits. 
"The cobra lives near towns where mice and rats abound." Well, I thought, that fits.
"Cobras have extensive tissues that store fat." Yup.
"The venom of cobras acts powerfully on the nerves of those it attacks." Clearly.
Maybe the president had it right. I was a major-league asp.
I puzzled over whether W. fancied himself the mongoose or the snake charmer in this allegory. Either way, I knew he would expect me to hiss a bit about his first 100 days.
It has been a strange start. Al Gore jokes he should be addressed as "Your Adequacy." But W. risks being "Your Inadequacy."
Men customarily build their presidencies around their strengths. W. has built his around his weaknesses.
His White House reminds me of the 1937 movie "Damsel in Distress," in which Fred Astaire has to frantically pirouette around Joan Fontaine to make up for the fact that she cannot dance.
Bush officials are always frantically pirouetting around W., making up for his stumbles and lacunae.
Last week, the president threw the planet into a turmoil when he went on "Good Morning America" and said he was willing to "do whatever it took to help Taiwan defend herself" against a Chinese attack, including sending American forces.
Obviously, Mr. Bush did not set out to change nearly 30 years of American policy on a morning chat show. But, sensitive about W.'s reputation as a featherweight, his aides did not want the president to have to admit he made a boo-boo and is a yo-yo who can't be trusted to carry on a brief discussion about his own policies with hard-hitting Charlie Gibson. (Hiss!)
So they tried to soften his statement while letting it stand, thereby enraging Beijing further.
On Friday night, Uncle Dick Cheney had to go on Larry King and explain in that reassuring basso that the Kid was not changing policy.
W.'s advisers tried to make him look more impressive in his first forays into diplomacy by keeping the big world leaders at bay and letting him hang out with lesser leaders he could talk to in Spanish.
So now we have a whole new alliance with Central and South American countries simply because W. feels more comfortable at what USA Today dubbed "amigo diplomacy." 
The ill-prepared president doesn't seem troubled by the state of his preparedness. There's no indication he's staying up late to make up the work. He isn't even aspiring to on-the-job training. The White House simply pretends that thoughtlessness is thoughtfulness, and that the president is governing when he is gaffe-ing. (Hiss!)
His team overreacts to his father's failings. Karl Rove, a.k.a. "Boy Genius," in W.'s nickname lexicon, is so assiduous about buttering up the right, which grew disillusioned with Bush pere, that he has alienated swing voters and Republican suburban women on the environment and abortion.
Lee Atwater, the bad-boy strategist for Bush Sr. who was a mentor to Mr. Rove, aimed to keep the right happy, but he never bowed and scraped to "extra-chromosome conservatives," as he and his boss called them.
It is ironic, given how intently they are shaping Bush II to avoid the errors of Bush I, that W.'s weakness in polls at the start is the same one that sunk his dad in the end.
In last week's ABC News/Washington Post poll, 51 percent said the president does not "understand the problems of people like you."
When the father got tarred for being out of touch, it was because of his patrician ways and because he was absorbed in his avocation, international affairs, while this country was hurting economically.
The son has a plain-spoken, colloquial style and homespun tastes. He runs from his gilded cradle, avoiding Yale and refusing to acknowledge Connecticut as his birthplace on a recent trip there. But he is not seen as a populist, either. He, too, is surrounded by wealthy older men. And they have given his economic and environmental policies a strong corporate aroma.
Air Force One is beginning to look like the company plane. (Hiss!)

HEADLINE: Labor Leaders Joining Forces In Opposition To Trade Plan 

BYLINE:  By STEVEN GREENHOUSE

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 26 

 BODY:
The nation's labor unions are joining with their international counterparts in an escalating campaign to fight President Bush's efforts to create a free trade zone throughout the Western Hemisphere.
 Over the past week, American labor leaders held protest rallies in five cities and met with Latin American union officials to battle the proposed trade zone, saying it would encourage corporations to move operations to areas with the worst wages and working conditions. 
 Calling the proposal "a Nafta clone," the A.F.L.-C.I.O. said it would urge millions of union members nationwide to join a campaign against the trade zone like those labor mounted against the China trade deal and the North American Free Trade Agreement. The labor federation's leaders voiced confidence that, working with environmental groups, they could persuade Congress to deny Mr. Bush the expedited negotiating authority he has said he needs to negotiate a hemispheric free trade agreement. 
Last weekend in Quebec, Mr. Bush and the heads of 33 other countries pledged to set up the Free Trade Area of the Americas, which would eliminate tariffs from the Canadian Arctic to the tip of South America.
"The Free Trade Area of the Americas looks to us as if it is modeled after Nafta," said Thea Lee, the A.F.L.-C.I.O.'s chief international economist. "We will oppose it vigorously if it continues along the same lines. As far as we can see, there appears to be a total absence of worker rights and environment standards."
Labor leaders from 34 countries in the Western Hemisphere met in Washington this week with John Sweeney, the federation's president, to map plans to fight the new free trade zone. That group, the Inter-American Regional Organization of Workers, issued a statement criticizing the trade zone, saying it would promote "a race to the bottom" in which corporations seek out countries that pay the lowest wages and have the worst working conditions.
Several members of Congress predicted that Mr. Bush would have as hard a time as President Bill Clinton did in gaining fast-track negotiating authority. That authority, which the Bush administration has called "trade promotion authority," makes it easier for presidents to negotiate trade accords because it bars Congress from amending them, allowing it only to vote up or down.
Largely because of labor's opposition, Mr. Clinton repeatedly failed to gain fast-track authority after 1994.
Representative Robert T. Matsui, a California Democrat who has often opposed organized labor on Nafta and fast track, said: "My read is that fast track is going to be extremely difficult to pass. I don't think much has changed since 1994. You still have the fundamental problem of how do you gather 218 votes in the House."
Administration officials said they would work hard to obtain fast-track authority. An official in the United States Trade Representative's Office said: "President Bush has made it clear that trade promotion authority is a priority for the administration. We are working to develop a broad coalition in support."
A big obstacle is that many Republicans from the Northeast and Midwest appear likely to oppose fast track. Predicting that at least two dozen Congressional Republicans would vote against it, Jack Quinn, a Republican from Buffalo who heads a coalition of union-friendly House Republicans, said: "To me, F.T.A.A. is an extension of Nafta, and in my opinion Nafta has been a failure."
Union officials vowed to do their utmost to block fast track and the proposed free trade zone unless President Bush agrees to incorporate strong labor and environmental protections in any trade accord. They said they doubted he would, and predicted that other countries would block such protections even if the President insisted on them.
Specifically, union leaders want the United States to be able to bring trade sanctions if a trading partner does not enforce its labor laws, like a minimum wage, or basic labor rights, like the right to form a union.
"The F.T.A.A., as it is presently drafted, is a disaster," Mr. Sweeney said. "From what we're seeing, it doesn't do anything meaningful on core labor standards and environmental concerns."
Robert B. Zoellick, the United States trade representative, said in Quebec that Mr. Bush wanted to try to improve labor standards and environmental conditions. "We're willing to consider a whole host of ways to do so," he said.
Mr. Zoellick has said that if labor rights are included in trade accords, labor violations should be punished with monetary penalties and not with trade sanctions, which he has suggested could be used for protectionist purposes. But labor federation officials argue that protections of labor rights will not have teeth unless countries face trade sanctions.

HEADLINE: Patience in Omaha Pays Off Again 

BYLINE:  By JOSEPH B. TREASTER

DATELINE: OMAHA, April 28 

 BODY:
For a couple of trying years, through his annual meeting here last spring, Warren E. Buffett's long record of finding great values in companies making soft drinks, razor blades and other everyday products failed to keep investors, friends and just about anyone he bumped into from saying that he really ought to get into those marvelous, highflying technology stocks.
 Shares in Berkshire Hathaway, his company, lost about half their value as the Nasdaq index, laden with technology, doubled and redoubled. But he insisted, to nearly everyone's disbelief, that he simply could not figure out how to handicap the high-tech stocks and their even trickier dot.com cousins. 
 But this year, more than 10,000 of Mr. Buffett's shareholders are gathered here to celebrate his vindication, and the vindication of the value investing style he personifies. With the Nasdaq index down about 59 percent from its peak last March, and the broader Standard & Poor's 500-stock index down 18 percent over the same period, Berkshire Hathaway has risen 12.8 percent since its shareholders met a year ago. 
Mr. Buffett was greeted with a standing ovation as he stepped onto a plain stage at the Civic Auditorium this morning. "People are really excited about him this year," said Diane Guerin, an Omaha writer who has owned Berkshire stock for several years. "Berkshire has held steady," she said, "whereas so many other stocks have tanked. We're grateful for that."
Value investors like Mr. Buffett are the investment world's discount shoppers, looking for reliable companies that are worth more than their stock prices reflect. Growth investors, on the other hand, have an eye for glamour, for companies, often in technology, with the most rapidly rising sales and profits, even if their shares sell at a premium.
"If you're the kind of person who will walk across the street to get a better price on a hotel room, you're probably a value investor," said Robert P. Miles, a Tampa, Fla., businessman who became so enamored of the Buffett style after a few years of owning Berkshire shares that he wrote a book, "101 Reasons to Own the World's Greatest Investment," which has just been published.
As technology stocks rocketed, some leading value investors, like Julian Robertson of Tiger Management, simply gave up on managing large pools of money. Some other major investors, like George Soros, reluctantly joined the technology parade, only to suffer big losses.
But now value is back. "A year ago it was the darkest moment before dawn for value investors," said Thomas M. Galvin, chief investment officer at Credit Suisse First Boston. "Tech stocks were at their peak, and it appeared that value managers were headed toward the burial ground. Now it's pretty clear they have been reborn and rectified."
The flow of investors' money demonstrates the trend. In February and March, money streamed out of mutual funds featuring Internet and technology stocks and began flowing into funds that generally follow a value investing philosophy. 
The value investments have been holding their own or even gaining a little. Of the 31 categories of mutual funds tracked by Lipper Inc., all lost money in the first three months of this year except value funds that hold relatively small companies.
Investors here, at the nation's largest shareholder meeting, basked in their good fortune as they were welcomed by Mr. Buffett, as always, to a jazz and wine reception (and sales opportunity) at Berkshire's sprawling Borsheim's jewelry store, and then went for ice cream at a Dairy Queen, another Berkshire company. 
"It's certainly a little more upbeat this year," said Jim Wiles, a retired General Motors executive.
Though such festive scripting, and Mr. Buffett's accomplished folksiness, guarantee a good time, last year the mood was strained. Even some of the loyalists at the meeting tactfully suggested to Mr. Buffett that he might need to update his style. This year, one investor generated a wave of applause by rising to thank Mr. Buffett for resisting the technology sector's lure.
Now some Wall Street wizards caution that the market might already be taking another turn that would sideline the value crowd once again. Jeffrey M. Applegate, the chief investment strategist at Lehman Brothers, sees evidence that technology and other growth stocks are once again gathering steam, to the detriment of value stocks. 
"When you have a situation in which interest rates are falling and price-earnings multiples are expanding," Mr. Applegate said, referring to the ratio of share price to earnings per share, "growth typically outperforms value. And that's what we've been seeing."
As for Mr. Buffett, whose Berkshire holdings are worth nearly $33 billion, it remains unclear whether he can continue delivering the gains that brought Berkshire Hathaway, which has never split its stock, from $27 a share in 1967 to a peak of more than $80,000 in 1998.
His largest investments, including Coca-Cola and Gillette, have been languishing far below their highs, troubled by poor sales and management shake-ups. And Berkshire's single largest unit, General Re, a leader in selling insurance to insurance companies, has been gushing losses since Mr. Buffett bought it for nearly $22 billion in 1998.
Mr. Buffett is keeping the faith with his insurance holdings, saying that insurance prices are rising, and with his name-brand stocks. But rather than invest in more stocks this year, he said, he is likely to hunt for entire businesses to buy.
Lately, he remarked, referring to a market he still regards as overheated, "the climate has not been friendly toward buying" stocks. "It is just astounding to me," he added, "how willing people are, during a bull market, to throw money around."
Last year, Mr. Buffett bought eight companies that produce enduring, if unexciting, things like bricks, carpets and cowboy boots. He paid $8 billion, 97 percent in cash.
"We remain awash in liquid assets and are both eager and ready for even larger acquisitions," Mr. Buffett wrote in Berkshire's annual report. "The larger the company, the greater will be our interest."
To warm shareholders up before the serious business began, Mr. Buffett presented, as he does each year, an hourlong film spoofing himself and his close friend Charles T. Munger, Berkshire's vice chairman. The film opened with Mr. Buffett strumming a ukelele. At one point, in one of many cameo appearances by famous friends, he was shown caddying for Tiger Woods.
Mr. Buffett kept the quips coming as he and Mr. Munger fielded questions from shareholders in what many financial experts regard as an incomparable seminar in value investing. As the two Berkshire leaders took their seats at a table on the stage, Mr. Buffett joked about their ages; he is 70 and Mr. Munger 77.
"I'm Warren," Mr. Buffett said by way of introduction. "He's Charlie. He can hear. I can see."
In the last year, Berkshire's stock beat the performance of the S.& P. 500 by nearly 16 percentage points. As much as anything, Mr. Buffett's strategy during the recent techno-craze was defensive, with benefits that became clear over the last year. "One of the cornerstones of the value approach," said Robert G. Hagstrom, who runs a Legg Mason value fund and has written several books about Mr. Buffett's investing style, "is a tendency to give back less than the market as a whole when things get bad." 
In more than 30 years of running Berkshire Hathaway, Mr. Buffett has never had a losing year, although he came close in 1999 with a gain of just half of 1 percent.
Jack and Betty Rhodes, a retired couple living in Peoria, Ill., started investing in Berkshire five years ago. Their idea was to treat Berkshire like a value-oriented mutual fund and use it, along with utility stocks, as a counterweight to their technology stocks.
The Rhodeses, who were attending their third annual meeting, said the investment had worked just as they planned. Over all, they say, their portfolio is down 17 percent this year, much less worse than it would have been without the Berkshire investment.
"Compared to the tech stocks," Mrs. Rhodes said, "you'd have to be euphoric about Berkshire."
Many here this weekend expressed a deep sense of relief and gratitude. "Everybody knows he's brought us through a very dangerous time in the markets," said Andrew Kilpatrick, a Birmingham, Ala., stockbroker and Berkshire shareholder who has also written a book on Mr. Buffett. "It took tremendous character to not say, 'O.K., I'll buy some tech to get everybody off my back.' With hindsight, now we can say he was right. He's in better shape than anybody else."

HEADLINE: MEDIA; 
Time Magazine in Europe IsNow More European

BYLINE:  By SUZANNE KAPNER 

DATELINE: LONDON, April 29 

 BODY:
Passengers boarding the Eurostar train to Paris from Waterloo station in London will be hard pressed not to notice a series of splashy billboard advertisements beginning in May that will introduce Time as Europe's newsmagazine.
 That may seem like an unlikely statement for such an American icon. Even though the flagship title of Time Inc., part of AOL Time Warner, has published a European edition since 1973, it is still perceived as an American magazine, said Donald Morrison, who has been co-editor of the European edition of Time since November with his wife, Ann. 
 The Morrisons, both 54 and longtime Time Inc. employees, aim to reposition the magazine by giving it more of a European sensibility and wooing younger readers and women. 
Three-quarters of Time Europe's current readers are men and over 35, something the editors hope to change with more business and technology coverage (a new addition: Start-Up of the Week) and by writing about fashion and design trends.
But by far the most ambitious of these changes is the cultivation of a European perspective. Research showed that readers wanted less about the United States and more about Europe, Mr. Morrison said.
And so a recent cover story on the human genome delved into the genetic differences between the Basques, the Spanish and the French and the similarities between Hungarians and surrounding Slavs, nuances largely lost on non-Europeans.
A profile of the French actor Fabrice Luchini, well-known only in his home country, drew praise from advertisers, who expressed surprise that an American magazine had picked up on such a French icon, according to Wil Merritt, Time Europe's president and publisher.
That is not to say that Time Europe ignores the big Hollywood stars. But when it does write about them, as in an April 2 profile of Penelope Cruz, it takes a decidedly European slant, describing her as an average 26-year-old European "on the move, leaving her homeland to further her career but retaining a strong connection to her Spanish roots."
The profile sits alongside the cover story of that same issue, "Generation Europe," which explores whether economically unified Europeans are developing a common culture.
Mrs. Morrison said the coming together of ideals, replacing postwar political squabbling, made appealing to a European reader possible.
Some of Time Europe's critics, and even a few supporters, question whether the Morrisons can thrive where others have failed, given the magazine's American heritage.
"It's like McDonald's trying to become an authentic French bistro," said Fareed Zakaria, editor of Newsweek International, which, he said, does not apologize for being an American newsmagazine. "We don't try to hide what we are."
Hugues Olivier Bores, chief operating officer of the watchmaker Patek Philippe, one of Time's advertisers, said he identified with the magazine's goal of attracting women and younger readers, something Patek Philippe is trying to accomplish with its own customers, but was less confident about Time's ability to distance itself from its American roots.
"Time has always been noted as giving the U.S. side of the story," said Mr. Bores, who is French. "Can an American magazine be the voice of Europe?"
Initial results show the strategy is making some headway. For the April 2 issue, which was the first with the changes, newsstand sales in Britain, France and Germany increased at least one-third, compared with the prior issue, though it was unclear how much of those gains were driven by a $10 million marketing campaign scheduled to run through September.
Time Inc. has spent liberally to bolster its international editions before. In 1996 it established regional editorial headquarters, tripling expenses for many departments. The Morrisons, fresh from Hong Kong, where Mr. Morrison was editor of Time Asia, and Mrs. Morrison editor of Asiaweek, another Time Inc. publication, are beneficiaries of that decision. "We never got complaints in Asia that Time was too American," Mr. Morrison said.
In Europe, where competition from local television and magazines is stiffer, Time will have to recruit more readers like Tony Evans, 28, a business director for Mindshare, a media-planning agency in London that places some advertisements with Time Europe, who said: "I always thought Time was too American, but now it seems to be written with more of a European viewpoint."

HEADLINE: The Internet Bubble Bursts on the Screen; 
Documentary Shows Brief Life Of a Dot-Com

BYLINE:  By JOHN SCHWARTZ 

 BODY:
Kaleil Isaza Tuzman walks into the office at the end of a very bad day. He is returning from yet another exhausting business trip, trying to save his struggling dot-com, GovWorks. The $60 million he helped raise for the company is burning away as if it were jet fuel, workers have been laid off and he has just dismissed a co-founder of the company, his childhood friend Tom Herman, after a bitter power struggle. Mr. Isaza Tuzman drops his bags, brings his hands together and begins to chant a Hindu prayer. On camera.
 It is one of many compelling scenes from "startup.com," a documentary by Chris Hegedus and D. A. Pennebaker -- who together made "The War Room," a documentary on the 1992 presidential campaign -- and a newcomer, Jehane Noujaim. 
 "Startup.com," which will open on May 11, is a text for fast times, a roller-coaster history of one dot-com's boom and bust that is appearing even before the general dot-com bust has played out. And the freshness of the events -- closing titles with updates on the main characters were revised just weeks before the release date -- makes "startup.com" seem less a documentary than an instant replay. 
The filmmakers call it "the rise and fall of the American dream," but the documentary is actually a squirmingly uncomfortable close-up of the Internet market mania of the last few years: a small tale that, under examination, tells a much bigger story.
It is only natural that the Internet bubble should burst on screen, since the roaring 90's turned business into entertainment.
As the stock market soared and millions of Americans became portfolio managers, the nation's televisions were tuned to CNBC and Internet executives became stars. 
It is not the first time that filmmakers have looked to the boardroom; some classic movies, including "Citizen Kane," are set in the world of business. The heyday was the 1950's, when Hollywood's biggest names made movies about making money. William Holden starred in "Executive Suite," Gregory Peck was "The Man in the Gray Flannel Suit" and Alec Guinness was "The Man in the White Suit." Directors have since returned to business but rarely with the attention to the details that made the earlier films so memorable. 
In the 1990's, however, the business of America seemed once again to be business. At times the lines blurred, as when the recently departed punk rock icon Joey Ramone wrote an ode to the CNBC financial reporter Maria Bartiromo. Even the TV idol William Shatner crooned for Priceline, sending up the ill-advised spoken-word rock album of his younger days. But when the good times soured, the Internet businesses looked more and more like reality TV, with precious few survivors and plenty of weakest links.
And that could be why the new film is such a vivid document of the times -- and why a documentary, rather than a fictional film, might have been the best way to capture their hypercaffeinated spirit.
The original vision of the GovWorks founders was not particularly grand: to improve the way people pay parking tickets. But in time, the company saw itself transforming citizens' relationship with government, improving services and making government accountable 24 hours a day. They also hoped to tap into a market of some $4 trillion in annual government fees.
The documentary was conceived in 1998 when Ms. Noujaim realized that her roommate, Mr. Isaza Tuzman, was leaving a lucrative, secure job at Goldman, Sachs for the promise of dot-com zillions. She had been planning to quit her own job as a producer at MTV to start a film in Egypt but realized she had an amazing story in the messy bedroom down the hall.
She took a camera that she had bought with grant money from Harvard, her alma mater, and began following Mr. Isaza Tuzman around as he cleaned out his desk at Goldman and began assembling the skeleton staff of the company that eventually became GovWorks. 
Shortly after Ms. Noujaim began filming Mr. Isaza Tuzman, mutual friends introduced her to Chris Hegedus, who was also hoping to make a documentary about a start-up company.
Ms. Hegedus said that she saw the entrepreneurs as rebels, "kind of right out of the 60's, where I came from."
Mr. Isaza Tuzman's story, she said, captivated her because "he had left this very structured corporate world, and it felt like a revolution."
When she began looking into Internet companies, "I wasn't exactly sure where Silicon Alley was," Ms. Hegedus said, but she was surprised to find that it resembled the downtown loft scene she of the early 1970's, when artists and rock stars lived in many of the same neighborhoods.
The dot-commers, she said, "were almost like the rock stars of the new age."
The women decided to collaborate. Each filmmaker saw a benefit in the partnership: Ms. Hegedus would be in at the very beginning of a promising company with almost total access to meetings and top executives at all hours. Ms. Noujaim would gain the experience of working with one of the best-known documentary teams in the world, Ms. Hegedus and Mr. Pennebaker.
"I felt incredibly lucky," Ms. Noujaim said. She recalled thinking, "This film is going to be seen if Chris and Penne get involved." 
With Mr. Pennebaker as producer, Ms. Hegedus and Ms. Noujaim shot some 400 hours of film over more than a year of 18-hour days, in meeting rooms, gyms, cars, elevators and bathrooms, even following Mr. Isaza Tuzman to the White House, where he participated in a conference on the New Economy. 
Ms. Hegedus joked to Ms. Noujaim, "You're doing Proust here."
But Ms. Noujaim responded, "No, I just don't know when to put the camera down." After all, they said, no one knew how the story would end.
It does not give away too much of the story to say that things do not end well. The exhilaration of the building phase gives way to the raw exhaustion of the breakdown -- and the ugly break between Mr. Isasa Tuzman and Mr. Herman, which ends, like so many conflicts today, in a slurry of letters, lawyers, cell phones and bile. 
The subjects, Mr. Isaza Tuzman and Mr. Herman, say they admire the film and the dedication of the filmmakers. Mr. Herman, 30, said it was "an honor" to receive this form of celluloid immortality. But he also complains that the movie minimizes his leadership role in the company and the technological hurdles his team successfully overcame; the software they developed at GovWorks was acquired by a another company and is still in use in cities like Boston, Memphis and New York. 
Mr. Isaza Tuzman, 29, said he was mortified to see that the filmmakers included scenes showing the toll that his round-the-clock work schedule took on his relationships with one girlfriend and then a second. "It's just excruciating to watch," he said.
Ms. Hegedus had her own observation. "When people do this, they really give us their lives; we're eternally grateful to them," she said. "If they see you're involved, and they see you take them seriously and respect them, they'll let you into their lives."
Both Mr. Herman and Mr. Isaza Tuzman, however, said that "startup.com" had sacrificed realism in the interest of telling a good story. "They focused in on conflict, which makes good drama," Mr. Herman said. 
As evidence of their continuing friendship, they point to a fact that seems incredible to anyone who has seen the film: Mr. Isaza Tuzman and Mr. Herman are back in business again, together. 
After going through the wrenching experience of seeing the assets of his company sold off, Mr. Isaza Tuzman realized that the residual value to be found in dying dot-coms is often lost because most bankruptcy experts do not really understand the nature of the businesses involved. In other words, he saw another market. So he called Mr. Herman out of retirement, and they teamed up once again to form the Recognition Group. 
The company helps its clients, which include the failing companies' financial backers, work through the legal and emotional maze of bankruptcy and near-bankruptcy. This time, they hope to change the way Americans fail. 
"Pain is the greatest teacher," Mr. Isaza Tuzman said. "It may sound schmaltzy, but I have a strong spiritual center. I felt that there's a reason for everything, including missteps."
The men said they had learned from their mistakes and that their new company had achievable goals and defined milestones -- and, not surprisingly, a tight focus on quickly making a profit. "If there's not a revenue-generating outcome for something you're working on, you're wasting your time," Mr. Herman said. 
Mr. Isaza Tuzman, displaying the forcefulness that led venture capitalists to give millions to his young team at GovWorks, said he hoped that others would similarly learn specific lessons of the dot-com boom and bust and not just dismiss it as some kind of mass hallucination.
"If people let this pendulum swing too far in the other direction and all of these extraordinary, visionary and gutsy people go back to exactly what they were doing before and are disillusioned with entrepreneurship," he said, "that's an enormous loss to the economy."

HEADLINE: ARTS ONLINE; 
Guggenheim's Latest Branch Is to Open In Cyberspace

BYLINE:  By MATTHEW MIRAPAUL 

 BODY:
The Solomon R. Guggenheim Foundation, which has been aggressively expanding its global network of museums, is planning to open a typically ambitious outpost on a new frontier: cyberspace.
 In an attempt to combine art and e-commerce, the Guggenheim is planning to open a Web site this fall that will offer a range of cultural content and services -- some of it free -- in a visually exciting environment that is said to go well beyond most conventional museum sites. To be called Guggenheim.com, the site will also provide access to resources at other institutions, including the State Hermitage Museum in St. Petersburg, Russia. 
 Museum Web sites have become increasingly sophisticated as their owners have recognized the value of the Internet as both a marketing medium and a virtual gallery. Still, with its far-reaching multidisciplinary approach, Guggenheim.com has the potential -- potential being the operative word in an environment known for failed efforts and broken alliances -- to become the first online theme park for the arts, albeit a playland with classier rides than most. Here, Cubist paintings could become the visual equivalents of the tilt-a-whirl.
Although it is clear that the relentlessly ambitious Guggenheim intends to establish its brand on the Internet with this online arts center, the site's full scope has not been revealed. Guggenheim.com officials declined to comment until the project is formally announced in several weeks, and a foundation spokesman said that Thomas Krens, the Guggenheim's director, was traveling and unavailable for comment. But details emerged in interviews with people who have seen the project and spoke on the condition of anonymity.
Plans for the site, scheduled to open in September, include high-quality digital reproductions of paintings, sculptures and other works from the Guggenheim museums in Manhattan, Venice, Berlin and Bilbao, Spain, as well as from partner institutions. In addition to the Hermitage, these include the Kunsthistorisches and Albertina museums in Vienna (the latter has a vast trove of drawings) and the Center for Art and Media Technology in Karlsruhe, Germany.
The Guggenheim site may also screen videos, exhibit other examples of new-media art and present concerts and performances. According to the museum's current plan, the online works will be supplemented by artists' biographies, scholarly commentary and educational information. All this is "for the serious researcher as well as the Sunday museumgoer," said one person who had seen a working version of the site.
Perhaps because the Guggenheim is so actively pursuing the concept of art as entertainment, it is pushing to make sure that the site has a dynamic appearance. Those who have seen it describe it as "flashy," "captivating" and "unlike anything else out there." One viewer said, "It has the feeling of a well-produced CD-ROM rather than a database."
There are certainly plans for commerce in Guggenheim.com, including a gift shop and the sale of educational materials. A travel-planning service is expected to provide revenue by offering cultural-tourism packages that might whisk art lovers from, say, Bilbao to Karlsruhe to St. Petersburg.
Additional funds are to come from sponsors, whose logos may appear in a corner of the computer screen in discreet acknowledgments similar to those used on public television.
The for-profit Guggenheim.com is to be separate from the Guggenheim Foundation, which is to maintain its tax-exempt, nonprofit status. Mr. Krens will be the site's chief executive, and Judith Cox, the Guggenheim's former deputy director and general counsel, will be chief operating officer. Hani Rashid, a partner in Asymptote Architecture, a New York firm, will be the chief creative officer. The site is to have a staff of 50 employees operating out of a Lower Manhattan office.
Like many e-commerce start-ups, Guggenheim.com is receiving some funds from venture capital concerns, including Softbank Venture Capital, Pequot Private Equity and G.E. Capital. Most of this money was secured before the e-commerce landscape changed from a sunny Monet to a desolate Hopper.
"Because they're so well-financed, they're in a position to do a good job, if they're capable of doing a good job," said one person who had seen a Guggenheim.com presentation. "It's not money that's going to stop them."
An obvious obstacle is the slow pace of museum bureaucracies. The Guggenheim already has a conventional Web site at guggenheim.org, and for years officials have promised to open the Guggenheim Virtual Museum, an online art showcase that may have planted the seeds for Guggenheim.com. Designed by Asymptote and meant to be as architecturally distinctive as the museum's Frank Lloyd Wright building on Fifth Avenue, the Guggenheim Virtual Museum has been finished for some time, but its premiere has been repeatedly postponed. 
The economic downturn may also pose problems for Guggenheim.com. Few businesses in any industry have succeeded online. And although cultural institutions are well stocked with valuable content that should attract upscale customers, their task is no easier. The extent of the challenge could be seen in last week's announcement by the Museum of Modern Art in Manhattan that it was rethinking its approach to the Internet now that the Tate Gallery in London had withdrawn from an e-commerce partnership. Some elements of the Modern's strategy are similar to those of Guggenheim.com.
Other culture-world players in e-commerce ventures have yet to flourish. Fathom.com, a distance-learning partnership among libraries and universities, spearheaded by Columbia, does not expect to turn a profit for two years, although a spokesman said its financial performance was in line with expectations. MuseumNetwork.com, an online entry point to a number of museums worldwide, suspended its consumer-sales business last year after just a few months and has scaled back its other activities.
"These things are much harder to do than people imagine," said David Bearman, president of Archives and Museum Informatics, a cultural-technology consulting company in Pittsburgh. "The problems that people are having as they try to create content from the digital assets of museums, special collections and libraries are evidence that this is an extremely difficult market to enter into successfully."
Mr. Bearman also cautioned that if museums start to resemble commercial entities too much, they may endanger their nonprofit status and their donor and government support.
"By trying to make a little bit of money out there in the market, museums are risking their fundamental standing within the society, which is much more important to them than a little bit of money today," he said.
The Guggenheim, which drew nearly 2.5 million visitors to its far-flung museums last year, is developing plans for additional sites in Las Vegas, Brazil and Lower Manhattan. Cyberspace provides limitless opportunities for expansion. But will museums find room to succeed there?
