HEADLINE: World Financial Officials Pledge to Work to Block New Crises Before They Happen 

BYLINE:  By JOSEPH KAHN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29 

 BODY:
Leaders of the International Monetary Fund, responding quickly to early rumblings from the Bush administration, pledged today to devote themselves to preventing financial crises around the world in an effort to avoid the multibillion-dollar bailouts that were a common occurrence in the late 1990's.
 After a weekend of talks among member nations of the fund and the World Bank, financial leaders made upbeat comments about the slumping world economy and promised to support a multibillion-dollar fight against infectious diseases in poor nations. 
 Protesters rallied today in the sunshine outside World Bank headquarters, demanding that wealthy nations forgive all debts of developing countries. But the turnout was far smaller than a year ago, when thousands of demonstrators stormed police barricades. 
Horst Kohler, the fund's managing director, pledged that his global lending agency would develop better "early warning systems" to identify and expose weaknesses in member nations before they become emergencies. He called the mission the central focus of his tenure at the fund, which began last summer.
The goal of increasing surveillance of member nations and more closely monitoring financial markets has been an often-repeated one since the I.M.F. failed to diagnose chronic weaknesses of Asian nations that led to worldwide financial turmoil in 1997.
But Mr. Kohler's decision to highlight the work as his new top priority appeared to be a politically astute attempt to blunt warnings from some Bush administration officials, who have sharply criticized the fund's bailouts in Asia and Russia. John Taylor, who has been selected to be the Treasury Department's top international official, has in the past called the fund ineffective and wasteful and advocated abolishing it. He has since backed off those views.
Addressing member nations of the fund today, Treasury Secretary Paul H. O'Neill said that the agency must become better at preventing, rather than responding to, crises. The United States is the fund's largest shareholder, but it does not control the policies of the 183-nation lender.
"I envision that the I.M.F., while sharpening its ability to respond to financial disruptions swiftly and appropriately, does so less frequently because it has succeeded in preventing crises from developing in the first place," Mr. O'Neill said in a statement to the fund's controlling oversight board.
Mr. Kohler echoed the theme in comments after the day's meetings. "The I.M.F. needs to work even harder to put crisis prevention at the heart of its activities," he said.
Mr. Kohler said the fund has set up a new capital markets group that will communicate more with private bankers and traders to spot potential difficulties early. It has started a pilot program with the World Bank to assess the stability of the financial sectors of member nations, he said.
While few disagree with the notion that the fund should seek to prevent crises from happening, the challenge is to identify -- and in some cases to expose -- weaknesses in public finances or banks of member nations before they undermine confidence.
Despite the focus on prevention, donor nations of the fund indicated broad support today for its two latest emergency rescue efforts -- Turkey and Argentina.
Officials said Turkey's $10 billion aid package would be finalized within days. Argentina made progress toward persuading the fund that its newest program of financial changes, developed by Domingo Cavallo, the nation's new economic minister, would lead to fresh growth while narrowing the nation's budget gap, fund officials said.
Treasury officials have indicated that they see Turkey as a test case of their insistence on accountability, warning Turkish leaders that they should not return to the fund for more money if their financial overhaul, which includes bank reforms, privatization and budget cutting, fails to restore investors' confidence.
Financial officials also said they had made progress generating support for a multibillion-dollar global effort to fight AIDS, malaria and tuberculosis. The effort, proposed by Italy, would dramatically increase spending on anti-disease programs by the World Bank and United Nations and would focus primarily on Africa.
"I think this absolutely must be done and will be done," Laurent Fabius, France's finance minister, said today. He said the goal is to raise as much as $10 billion to support research and to make drugs available in nations where infectious diseases are prevalent. The amount is many times greater than is devoted to the fight against the diseases today.
Mr. O'Neill sounded a skeptical note about how useful such an effort would be in Africa, which lacks the infrastructure to administer a widespread disease treatment program, a senior Treasury official said. But the broad goal of starting such an effort appeared in the ministers' communique today, and Treasury officials have not ruled out participation by the United States.

HEADLINE: Giuliani Plans To Order Shift In Rent Board 

BYLINE:  By ERIC LIPTON with BRUCE LAMBERT 

 BODY:
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, frustrated with what he considered excessive rent increases imposed last year on tenants of the city's 1 million rent-stabilized apartments, has decided to replace the chairman of the Rent Guidelines Board just as it begins debating the size of this year's increases, administration officials said yesterday.
 Deputy Mayor Robert M. Harding said Mr. Giuliani and other officials "were disappointed" after the nine-member board, in a 5-to-4 vote last June, approved the largest rent increase since 1996. Now, before this year's vote, they want to replace the chairman, Edward S. Hochman, and to fill two vacancies, Mr. Harding said. 
 Steven Sinacori, 30, a lawyer in private practice who lives in Queens, was once an aide in the office of Mr. Giuliani's chief of staff and more recently was on the staff of the city's Board of Standards and Appeals, will be named chairman, Mr. Harding said.
Mr. Hochman, 48, of Manhattan, who has been chairman since the start of the Giuliani administration in 1994, said yesterday that he had not been notified that he was being replaced. He said he was prepared to preside today over the board's first official hearing on this year's rent increase proposals, scheduled to start at 9:30 a.m. at 9 Metrotech Center in Downtown Brooklyn.
"I don't know whether it is retaliatory or whatever else because I don't know whether it is true," said Mr. Hochman, a corporate lawyer, adding that he was weary after seven years on the board and had "mumbled about" wanting to step down.
For much of the Giuliani administration, low inflation and steady fuel prices translated into modest rent increases from the guidelines board -- and correspondingly little political fallout. But last year, complaining of higher fuel-related bills for heat and hot water, landlords pressed for much larger increases.
Their requests were met by protests from groups representing the 2.3 million tenants of the city's rent-stabilized apartments. 
Despite Mr. Giuliani's publicly stated objections, the board approved a 4 percent increase for one-year leases and 6 percent for two-year renewals, slightly higher than the mayor had sought. 
This year, tenant groups are demanding a rent freeze. But landlords, again citing high fuel costs, have asked for a 9 percent rise for one-year leases and 13 percent for two-year renewals, which would be the largest increase in two decades.
The board is set to take a preliminary vote on May 9, and a final vote in June. Increases would take effect only for leases signed after Oct. 1.
The mayor appoints all nine members of the Rent Guidelines Board. Two are designated as tenant representatives, two as representatives of property owners and five as representatives of the public at large. Mr. Hochman was chosen as a public representative. Service on the board is considered part time. Members are paid $100 for each day the board meets; the chairman gets $125.
Given the extremely close votes of the last two years -- the board also voted 5 to 4 in 1999 -- replacing Mr. Hochman and naming two new public representatives to the board could shift the tenor of the panel.
Mr. Hochman, long a target of criticism by tenant advocates, has been the subject of death threats. Shortly after he joined the board, furious tenants followed him to the subway after a meeting and threatened to throw him onto the tracks. He sometimes loses his own temper at board meetings, yelling back at tenants who curse or insult him.
Mr. Hochman said yesterday that at times he felt like a dartboard, charged with enforcing what he considers a flawed rent-stabilization law. "We are not a social agency for either side," he said. "The law basically says, 'Prevent tenants from being hit with unconscionable rent increases, while at the same time make sure -- in this vague term -- that landlords make a 'fair and reasonable' return.' "
Even before word circulated yesterday that Mr. Hochman was going to be asked to resign, Kenny Schaeffer, vice chairman of the Metropolitan Council on Housing, a tenants' group, called for Mr. Hochman's ouster. A protest rally is set for tonight.
"Ed Hochman has failed to protect the one million households living in stabilized apartments from oppressive rents," Mr. Schaeffer said during a news conference on the steps of City Hall, which was also attended by City Comptroller Alan G. Hevesi, a Democratic candidate for mayor. "The Rent Guidelines Board has been asleep at the wheel."
Mr. Hevesi said that even with increases in fuel and other costs in the last two years, last year's rent increase and the one sought by landlords this year were not justified. He and Mr. Schaeffer cited a Rent Guidelines Board study released recently that concluded that landlords netted an average of $314 per month in 1999 for each rent-stabilized apartment they own, the highest monthly profit since the board began tracking profits in 1989.
"At a time when landlords are reaping record profits, such an enormous increase would be grossly unfair," Mr. Hevesi said in a letter dated last week to Mr. Hochman, objecting to a possible 13 percent increase.
Jack Freund, executive vice president of the Rent Stabilization Association, the city's largest landlord group, defended the size of the increase being sought. Even before he knew of Mr. Hochman's pending departure, Mr. Freund expressed some wariness about board members the mayor might appoint.
"What concerns us is that the mayor, who has expressed his desire to have the board do his bidding, may well fill those spots with members who are more likely to do his bidding," Mr. Freund said late last week. "Therefore, we'll have not a rational process but a political process." 
Appointing a new chairman and filling the two vacancies pose other issues. Newcomers will be voting without having heard testimony and staff reports about landlord costs and profitability, housing availability, rent levels and tenant income. They will also have missed the chance to question speakers, hear board discussions and join in the debate. "There's no real way for them to catch up," said Michael McKee, associate director of New York State Tenants and Neighbors Coalition, a tenant advocacy group.
City officials and tenant advocates dismissed suggestions yesterday that the rent increases could be anywhere near 13 percent. An index of landlord costs, which the board uses to help decide increases, jumped by 8.7 percent in the last year, but that is only one of about four indicators considered by the board, Mr. Hochman said.
Mr. McKee said, "The whole buzz about 9 and 13 percent is a fraud, a stalking horse, to enable them to impose an unjust 6 and 8 and hope tenants breathe a sigh of relief." 
Mr. Sinacori could not be reached for comment yesterday.
Mr. McKee said he knew little about Mr. Sinacori, whom Mr. Harding described as "someone with a good understanding of the city government, a good understanding of real estate and the market in the city."

HEADLINE: History Collides With Contrition as Jewish Group Honors a German 

BYLINE:  By TAMAR LEWIN 

 BODY:
In a quiet watershed of Jewish philanthropy, the honoree at a UJA-Federation benefit dinner next month will be Thomas Middelhoff, the chief executive of Bertelsmann, a German media conglomerate that published Nazi propaganda for Hitler's army. 
 Those who chose Mr. Middelhoff to be honored at the Steven J. Ross dinner on May 15 stress that Mr. Middelhoff was born after the war and has investigated Bertelsmann's past and made what reparations he could. 
 "Of course we don't forget the Holocaust, but this is a man who was born in 1953, and has taken the company in a different direction," said Howard J. Rubenstein, co-chairman of the UJA-Federation of New York's media, communications and entertainment division, which puts on the annual Ross dinner at the Waldorf-Astoria. "Israel recognizes Germany now. It's been more than 50 years. We thought about it, and we thought it was time. And the reaction I've heard has been positive. We've sold way over $1 million in tickets." 
But whatever the financial rewards, the decision to honor Mr. Middelhoff has raised difficult questions about when a corporate executive should be held responsible for his company's past; when it is time to forgive, if not forget; and whether the Jewish community is ready to move into a new relationship with German companies. 
"How long do you carry this on?" said Laurence Tisch, co-chairman of the Loews Corporation and a prominent Jewish philanthropist. "It's been 50, 60 years. You don't want to forget, but at some point, you do want some closure. I don't know when, in a big public company that has changed management three times, you should hold the C.E.O. responsible for the past. As I understand it, this is a company that's been attempting, through charity work, to make up for the past. I understand the concerns, but this doesn't bother me." 
But many American Jews, especially the children of Holocaust survivors, still carry a deep anger toward German companies that profited from the war: they would not dream of buying a Krups coffee grinder or a BMW. Some say that 50 years is nowhere near time enough to let go of that anger -- and that it is an outrage for a Jewish organization to give the head of such a company a humanitarian award, a decision they see as reflecting financial concerns at the expense of moral ones. 
"Try me in 5,000 years, and maybe we'll be ready to start talking," said Melvin Jules Bukiet, the editor of a forthcoming anthology of writings by the children of Holocaust survivors. "They should be ashamed of themselves at UJA. Are we allowing ourselves to be purchased? Of course we are, and it's an outrage. These are the Jews, the people predicated upon memory. At Passover every year, we say it's as if every one was there at Sinai. Perhaps they'd take it more seriously if we said that everyone was at Auschwitz." Healing, he said, is another word for forgetting -- and many in the second generation, like him, "prefer the open wound to closure." 
Some of those old enough to remember the war see the UJA honor to Mr. Middelhoff as a bitter dishonoring of the Jews killed during the war. 
"I don't think that people like Mr. Middelhoff should be held responsible for what happened, but I can't see honoring someone connected with that kind of past," said Gwendolyn Jacobs, an 83-year-old New Yorker. "It's a desecration, and I just think it's terrible for the UJA to do it. During the war, we did not do enough, fast enough, to react to the horrors. We didn't do enough then, and if we accept this kind of thing, again we're not doing enough." 
One prominent UJA donor, Matthew Bronfman, was so bothered by the plan to honor Mr. Middelhoff that he voiced his unhappiness to UJA officials, according to those involved in planning the dinner. Mr. Bronfman did not return phone calls seeking comment. Others familiar with the situation, however, said that the rest of the Bronfman family supported the choice of Mr. Middelhoff.
The $1,000-a-plate Ross dinner, at which Matthew Bronfman's brother Edgar Bronfman Jr. was a previous honoree, attracts some of the most prominent corporate and media figures in New York. This year, Tom Brokaw, the NBC News anchor, will be the master of ceremonies and Stephen M. Case, the chairman of AOL Time Warner, will present the award. 
Elie Wiesel, a writer and Holocaust survivor, will deliver the keynote speech. Last fall, Random House, which is owned by Bertelsmann, pledged $1 million to the Holocaust Survivors' Memoir Project, for which Mr. Wiesel is the honorary chairman. Mr. Wiesel said he agreed to speak because he trusted Mr. Middelhoff. "While it was in the end Random House that gave the money for the survivors' memoirs, the agreement was with Bertelsmann, and it was Middelhoff who made the commitment," Mr. Wiesel said. "He also called me, and he really seemed very upset when the stories of Bertelsmann's past came out. We discussed it at length, and my advice was to come clean, to let everything be open. And I believe he has done that." 
Not long after Mr. Middelhoff became chief executive in 1998, critical stories about Bertelsmann's war history appeared in The Nation magazine and a Swiss magazine. Mr. Middelhoff then created an independent commission, headed by the historian Saul Friedlaender, to look into the company's past. In January 2000, the commission found that Bertelsmann had not opposed Hitler, as the company had previously claimed. Rather, it said, the company had ties to the Nazis, and was the largest supplier of reading material, including Nazi propaganda, to the German military. Three months later, Bertelsmann announced that it would contribute to the German fund to compensate workers used as slave labor in the Nazi era. 
"What more can they do that they haven't done?" asked Kenneth J. Bialkin, a New York lawyer who is active in Jewish causes. "Should we wake up every morning and reindict? No one can ever undo what was done, but I don't think we can afford the luxury of hating, of self-pity about the past. There are people whose minds and hearts are set in a time warp of 50 years ago. But people, and companies, move on. The State of Israel has plenty of real enemies today. We need economic, financial, political and diplomatic support wherever we can get it."
